تعلق القائلون بأن التكوين والمكون واحد بشبه سمعية وشبه عقلية. فأما الشبه السمعية التي تعلقوا بها فمنها قوله تعالى: هَذَا خَلْقُ اللَّهِ فَأَرُونِ ماذا خلق الَّذِينَ مِن دُونِهِ فاستدل سبحانه وتعالى على توحده بالإلهية دون غيره بأفعاله التي أوجدها بقدرة بعد العدم، وأطلق عليها اسم الخلق، ثم أطلق اسم المصدر وإن كان جائزا على المفعول، كما يقال لمقدور الله تعالى: هذا قدرته، إلا أن ذلك مجاز لا يصار إليه إلا عن دليل، وفي القدرة إجماع ولا إجماع فيما نحن فيه. وقوله تعالى: إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ إلى قوله : رَبَّنَا مَا خَلَقْتَ هَذَا بطلا وفيه أدلة من وجوه أحدها: أنه جعل في خلق السماوات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار آيات وعلامات على وحدانيته لذوي العقل ليستدلوا بما فيها من علامات الحدوث على أن له محدثا أحدثه، وإنما يستدل على ذلك بمفعولاته لا بصفاته التي هي غير مرئية لنا، وما دل على الصانع فهو مصنوعه. والآخر أنه قال : وَيَتَفَكَّرُونَ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وإنما يكون التفكر فيه بالاستدلال بالصنع على الصانع، وجعل محل التفكر خلق السماوات والأرض، والعاقل يتفكر فيما يشاهد من أفعاله ليستدل به على الفاعل، فدل على أنه أراد به مفعولاته ومخلوقاته، وسماها خلقا دل على أن الخلق مخلوق. والآخر أنه قال : رَبَّنَا مَا خَلَقْتَ هَذَا بَطِلًا وهو إشارة إلى الخلق أيضا فإنه تعالى قال في أول الآية: إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السمواتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ثم قال في آخر الآية : وَيَتَفَكَرُونَ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ . فلو كان هذا الاسم مجازا للمخلوقات لما كرر هذا الاسم عليه. إذ ليس من شأن العرب التكرار في المجازات، وقوله تعالى: يَخلُقَكُمْ فِي بُطُونِ أُمَّهَتِكُمْ خَلْقًا مِنْ بَعْدِ خَلْقٍ فجعل خلقا بعد خلق والقديم لا يجوز أن يتأخر في الوجود حتى يكون قبله غيره. وقوله تعالى: أَمْ جَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاة خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ فتشبه الْخَلْق عَلَيْهِمْ قُلْ اللَّهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهُوَ الْوَاحِدُ الْقَهَّارُ يعني خالق كل خلق، وقال تعالى: ثُمَّ أَنشَأْنَهُ خَلْقًا آخَرَ وقوله تعالى: أَمَّن يَبْدَأُ الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ فجعل للخلق ابتداء وإعادة وكل واحد منهما دليل الحدوث. وقال تعالى : لَخَلْقُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ أَكْبَرُ مِنْ خَلْقِ النَّاسِ وما وصف بالقدم لا يوصف بالأكبر والأصغر. وقوله تعالى خبرا عن إبليس عليه اللعنة: وَلَا آمُرَهُمْ
Those who say that takwin (bringing into existence) and the mukawwan (thing brought into existence) are one have attached themselves to transmitted proofs (shubah sam'iyya) and rational proofs. As for the transmitted proofs they have attached themselves to, among them is His saying, the Exalted: 'This is the creation of Allah, so show me what those other than Him have created.' So He, glorified and exalted, argued for His oneness in divinity without other than Him by His acts which He brought into existence by power after non-existence, and He applied the name 'khalq' (creation) to them, then He applied the name of the masdar (infinitive) although it is permissible for the maf'ul (object), just as it is said for the predestined thing of Allah, the Exalted: 'this is His power,' except that that is a metaphor (majāz) which is not resorted to except with evidence, and there is consensus (ijmā') regarding power, but there is no consensus in what we are discussing. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth...' up to His saying: 'Our Lord, You have not created this in vain.' In it there are proofs from several aspects: one of them is that He made in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of night and day signs and indicators of His oneness for those of intellect, so that they might infer by what is in them of signs of temporality that He has an originator who originated it, and one infers that only by His effects (maf'ulāt), not by His attributes which are invisible to us, and what indicates the Maker is His made thing (masnū'). And the other is that He said: 'And they reflect upon the creation of the heavens and the earth,' and reflection upon it is only by inferring the Maker from the making, and He made the locus of reflection the creation of the heavens and the earth, and the rational person reflects upon what he witnesses of His acts to infer the Agent, so it indicates that He intended by it His effects and His created things, and He called them 'khalq', indicating that 'al-khalq' is the created thing (makhluq). And the other is that He said: 'Our Lord, You have not created this in vain,' and it is an indication to 'al-khalq' as well, for He, the Exalted, said at the beginning of the verse: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth,' then He said at the end of the verse: 'And they reflect upon the creation of the heavens and the earth.' So if this name were a metaphor for the created things, He would not have repeated this name for them, for it is not the way of the Arabs to repeat in metaphors. And His saying, the Exalted: 'He creates you in the wombs of your mothers, creation after creation' – He made 'khalq' after 'khalq', and the eternal (al-qadīm) cannot be delayed in existence such that something else precedes it. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Or have they attributed to Allah partners who created like His creation, so the creation became confused for them? Say: Allah is the Creator of everything, and He is the One, the Subduer' – meaning the Creator of every creation. And He said, the Exalted: 'Then We produced him as another creation.' And His saying, the Exalted: 'Or who begins the creation and then repeats it?' – He made for 'al-khalq' a beginning and a repetition, and each one of them is an evidence of temporality. And He said, the Exalted: 'The creation of the heavens and the earth is greater than the creation of mankind' – and what is described by eternity (qidam) cannot be described as greater or smaller. And His saying, the Exalted, relating the story of Iblis, upon him be curse: 'And I will not command them...' [incomplete]
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage presents the transmitted proofs (shubah sam'iyya) used by those who argue that takwin (the act of bringing into existence) is identical to the mukawwan (the thing brought into existence). The author cites multiple Qur'anic verses and explains how each one indicates that the term 'khalq' refers to the created object, not to a separate eternal attribute. The first proof is from Surah Luqmān: 'This is the creation of Allah, so show me what those other than Him have created.' Allah applies 'khalq' to His acts (af'āl) that He originated after non-existence, using the masdar (infinitive) form, which could be a metaphor for the maf'ul (object), but such metaphor requires evidence, and there is no consensus here unlike the case of 'qudra' (power). The second proof is from the āyat of Surah Āl-Imrān: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth...' The author extracts three arguments from this verse: (a) Allah made the act of creation (khalq) a sign of His oneness, and one infers the Maker from His effects, not His invisible attributes. (b) The verse says 'they reflect upon the creation of the heavens and the earth' – reflection is on the created things (masnū'āt) to infer the Maker. (c) The repetition of the word 'khalq' at the beginning and end of the passage shows it is not a metaphor, because Arabs do not repeat metaphors. The third proof is from Surah Az-Zumar: 'He creates you in the wombs of your mothers, creation after creation.' This sequence of 'khalq' after 'khalq' cannot apply to an eternal attribute (which is not subject to temporal sequence). The fourth proof is from Surah Ar-Ra'd: 'Or have they attributed to Allah partners who created like His creation...' Here 'khalq' is used for both the act and the object, and the verse says 'Allah is the Creator (Khāliq) of every creation (khalq).' The fifth proof is from Surah Al-Mu'minūn: 'Then We produced him as another creation' – showing 'khalq' as a new originated thing. The sixth proof is from Surah An-Naml: 'Or who begins the creation and then repeats it?' – 'khalq' has a beginning and a repetition, indicating temporality. The seventh proof is from Surah Ghāfir: 'The creation of the heavens and the earth is greater than the creation of mankind' – if 'khalq' were eternal, it could not be described as greater or smaller. Finally, the eighth proof is from the story of Iblīs: 'And I will not command them...' (the verse is incomplete but implies that Iblīs's act of misguidance is created). All these proofs aim to establish that the term 'khalq' in the Qur'an refers to the created entities, thus supporting the view that takwin is identical to the mukawwan.

Key Takeaway

What would change in your heart if you truly believed that? Choose one trial you are going through and consciously surrender it to Allah's decree this week.

Certainty and Metaphor in Theological Evidence
Refuting the Ash'ari use of metaphorical verses as certain proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why metaphorical interpretations (majaz) cannot serve as definitive proof in matters of belief (i'tiqadiyyat) according to the Maturidi school.

Core Idea
Certainty demands certainty; probability has no place in theology.

The Ash'aris often relied on verses like 'This is the creation of Allah' to argue that 'khalq' is an eternal divine attribute. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by exposing a critical flaw: metaphorical language cannot produce the certainty required for theological doctrines. He distinguishes between legal rulings, where predominant opinion suffices, and doctrinal matters, which demand absolute certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari objection
Read the passage and note how the Ash'ari uses the verse 'This is the creation of Allah' to claim that 'khalq' is an attribute. Understand that he accepts the linguistic possibility of masdar-maf'ul substitution but calls it a metaphor requiring evidence.
The Ash'ari position is that the verse is metaphorical (majaz) and requires external proof.
2
Understand the limitation of majaz
Al-Nasafi argues that metaphor does not yield certain knowledge (ilm qat'i). He explains that even if evidence might be present, the mere possibility of metaphor creates doubt and negates certainty.
Probability (ihtimal) is incompatible with certain knowledge.
3
Distinguish i'tiqadiyyat from ahkam
Notice the key distinction: in legal rulings (ahkam), predominant opinion (ghalib al-ray) is acceptable, but in matters of belief (masa'il i'tiqadiyya), only certain proof suffices. This is the Maturidi position.
This distinction is central to the argument.
4
See the self-defeating conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that the opponent's reliance on these verses is futile: if rational proof is brought, the verses are superfluous; if not, the argument fails. Either way, the opponent is refuted.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الشبه السمعية للأشعرية
ثم نشتغل بالجواب عن شبهة السمعية لنتفرغ للمحاجة العقلية فنقول : مما أنكرت على من يقول لك أن المراد بقوله : هَذَا خَلْقُ اللَّهِ فَأَرُونِي مَاذَا خَلَقَ الَّذِينَ مِن دُونِهِ وغيره من الآيات، هذا مخلوق الله إذ لا وجه لك إلى إنكار جواز إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول في اللغة، كما في العلم والقدرة إذ هما يذكران ويراد بهما ما يتعلقان به من المعلوم والمقدور؟ فإن قلت : هذا مجازا فلا يصار إليه إلا بعد على قولك وهو مذهب الأشعري قيام الدليل قيل: إن للمجاز لا يكون موجبا للعلم قطعا ويقينا، وإن لم يقم بعد دليل المجاز لجواز أن يقوم، ولهذا نقول بالوقف في الآية العامة وإن لم يقارنها دليل التخصيص لما أن الاحتمال ينافي العلم ، فأما إذا كان إحدى الجهتين أرجح من الأخرى فقد يوجب علم الرأي فيجوز الاحتجاج به في مسائل الأحكام إذ هي تثبت بغالب الرأي، فأما في المسائل الاعتقادية التي لا وجه إلى القول بثبوتها إلا بإقامة دليل موجب للعمل قطعا فلا وجه إلى التعلق به وهو مذهب شيخنا أبي منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله
فلا وجه لك إذا إلى التعلق بهذه الآيات المحتملة للمجاز في محل النزاع، إلا إذا أقمت الدلالة على نفي احتمال المجاز، ولا دلالة على ذلك معك فإذا قلت إن الدلائل المعقولة تدل عليه فقد تركت الاحتجاج بالآية واشتغلت بالمعقول، وإن صح دليلك فقد وقعت لك الغنية عن التعلق بهذه الآيات، وإن لم يصح بطل احتجاجك بالآيات فكيفما دارت القصة ظهر سفهك في التعلق بهذه الآيات.
حمل الكلام على المجاز جائز بالدليل
على أنا نقيم الدلالة على أن المراد من الآيات هو المخلوق دون الخلق بطريق المجاز، وحمل الكلام على المجاز جائز بالدليل فمن الدلائل ما أقمنا من الدلالة السمعية التي لا مجال للشبهة في إبطال الاحتجاج بها على ما سبق ذكره، ونقيم بعد هذا الدلائل العقلية - إن شاء الله تعالى على أن قولك: إن إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول مجاز فلا يصار إليه إلا بدليل كلام فاسد جدا لأنك إذا أنكرت أن لفظة الخلق ليست بمصدر والمخلوق ليس بمفعول، وذكر الخلق والمراد به المخلوق ليس بطريق إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول بل هو موضع في أصل اللغة للمخلوق. فقد نادى عليك جميع أهل اللغة بالإنكار، وظهر جهلك بلسان العرب ومواصفاتهم وموازينهم وصيغاتهم التي استعملوها لتفاهم ما في الضمائر بها، وأحد لا يمكنه إنكار هذا ، وطريق سلفك أنهم يقولون : إن الخلق إن كان في اللغة مصدرا إلا أنه ينبئ عن معنى ذلك المعنى راجع إلى غير المفعول، وأنه ليس بمعنى وراءه كالوجود مع الموجود سواء ، فمن حقك أن تقول ذكر الخلق وأراد به المصدر إلا أن المصدر ها هنا راجع إلى ذات المخلوق، لاستحالة أن يكون غيره، وخصمك يقول : إن المصدر ها هنا صفة الله تعالى قائمة به والمخلوق ما يتعلق بتلك
The refutation of the sam'iyyah objections of the Ash'aris. Then we occupy ourselves with the answer to the sam'iyyah objection to free ourselves for rational argument, and we say: What do you deny from one who says to you that the intended meaning of His statement 'This is the creation (khalq) of Allah, so show me what those besides Him have created' and other verses is 'this is the created thing (makhluq) of Allah'? For you have no basis to deny the permissibility of placing the verbal noun (masdar) in the position of the object (maf'ul) in language, as in 'knowledge' (al-ilm) and 'power' (al-qudra), since these two are mentioned and what is intended by them is that to which they relate, i.e., the known and the empowered. If you say, 'This is a metaphorical usage (majaz), so it is not resorted to except with (the establishment of) evidence (dalil),' according to your statement, which is the doctrine of al-Ash'ari, it is said: Indeed, the metaphorical usage does not necessitate certain and definitive knowledge (al-ilm qat'an wa yaqinan), even if the evidence for the metaphor has not been established, because it is possible that it might be established. For this reason we say that there is suspension (waqf) regarding the general verse even if it is not accompanied by evidence of specification (takhsis), because probability (ihtimal) negates knowledge. As for when one of the two aspects is more preponderant (arjah) than the other, it may necessitate opinion-based knowledge (ilm al-ray'), and it is permissible to use it as evidence in matters of rulings (ahkam), since these are established by predominant opinion (ghalib al-ray). As for matters of belief (masa'il i'tiqadiyya), which have no basis for being established except by adducing evidence that necessitates certainty (qat'an), there is no basis for relying on it. This is the doctrine of our teacher Abu Mansur al-Maturidi - may Allah have mercy on him. So you have no basis to rely on these verses that are susceptible to metaphorical interpretation in the point of dispute, unless you adduce evidence negating the possibility of metaphorical interpretation, and you have no such evidence. If you say that rational proofs indicate it, then you have abandoned using the verse as evidence and engaged in rational argument. If your proof is sound, then you have no need to rely on these verses; and if it is unsound, then your argument from the verses is invalid. However the matter turns, your foolishness in relying on these verses becomes apparent. Carrying the speech to metaphorical interpretation is permissible with evidence. Moreover, we will adduce evidence that the intended meaning of the verses is the created thing (makhluq) and not 'creation' (khalq) via metaphorical interpretation (majaz), and carrying speech to metaphorical interpretation is permissible with evidence. Among the evidences are the sam'iyyah evidences we have adduced, which leave no room for doubt in invalidating the argument by means of them, as has been mentioned earlier. And after this we will adduce rational proofs - if Allah wills - that your statement: 'Indeed, placing the verbal noun in the position of the object is metaphorical, so it is not resorted to except with evidence' is utterly corrupt speech. Because if you deny that the word 'khalq' is a verbal noun and that 'makhluq' is an object, and that mentioning 'khalq' while intending 'makhluq' is not by way of placing the verbal noun in the position of the object, but rather it is a place in the original language for the created thing, then all experts in language would cry out in denial against you, and your ignorance of the language of the Arabs, their idioms, their standards, and their morphological patterns that they used to communicate what is in the minds becomes apparent. No one can deny this. And the way of your predecessors is that they say: Although 'khalq' in language is a verbal noun, it indicates a meaning that refers to something other than the object, and it is not a meaning behind it, like existence (wujud) and the existent (mawjud) are the same. So it is your right to say that he mentioned 'khalq' and intended by it the verbal noun, except that the verbal noun here refers to the essence of the created thing (dhat al-makhluq), due to the impossibility that it be other than that. And your opponent says: The verbal noun here is an attribute of Allah Most High subsisting in Him, and the created thing is that which relates to that attribute.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari school's sam'iyyah (scriptural) objections regarding the interpretation of the word 'khalq' in the Quran. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, presents two main arguments. First, he addresses the claim that verses like 'This is the creation of Allah' can be interpreted as 'this is the created thing of Allah' by way of placing the verbal noun (masdar) in the position of the object (maf'ul). He notes that the Ash'ari opponent accepts this linguistic possibility but argues that it is a metaphorical usage (majaz) that requires evidence. Al-Nasafi responds that metaphorical usage does not produce certain knowledge (al-ilm al-qat'i), and that in matters of belief (masa'il i'tiqadiyya) only certain evidence is acceptable, unlike in legal rulings where predominant opinion suffices. Thus, the opponent cannot rely on such verses without eliminating the possibility of metaphorical interpretation. The author then concludes that the opponent's position is self-defeating: if rational evidence is adduced, the verses become superfluous; if not, the argument fails. Second, al-Nasafi introduces a stronger linguistic argument: the word 'khalq' in the Arabic language is originally a noun meaning 'the created thing' (makhluq), not a verbal noun used metaphorically. He argues that the opponent's claim that using 'khalq' to mean 'makhluq' is a metaphor is false because in the original language it is a direct lexical term for the created thing. He supports this by appealing to the consensus of Arabic scholars and by analogy with terms like 'wujud' and 'mawjud', which are synonymous. The author further explains that even if 'khalq' is considered a verbal noun, it necessarily refers to the created thing because it is impossible for it to refer to something other than that. The opponent's counter-claim that 'khalq' is an eternal divine attribute is briefly mentioned but not refuted in the given text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'khalq' in verses like 31:11 must be interpreted as the attribute of creation, not the created thing.
Correction: Nasafi argues that even if metaphorical, it cannot serve as certain proof for doctrine; later he shows it is not metaphorical at all.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi argue that metaphorical usage (majaz) cannot be relied upon in matters of belief (masa'il i'tiqadiyya)?
A) Because metaphorical usage is forbidden in general.
B) Because metaphorical usage does not produce certain and definitive knowledge.
C) Because the Quran contains no metaphors.
D) Because the Ash'aris reject all metaphorical interpretations.

Key Takeaway
In matters of belief, only evidence that yields certainty (qat') is acceptable; probability (zann) from metaphor is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse whose meaning is not immediately clear, are you quick to adopt a metaphorical interpretation that fits your preferences, or do you suspend judgment until certainty is established? How does this affect your certainty in Allah's attributes?
?
In your daily life, do you apply the same level of scrutiny to your theological beliefs as you do to your legal practices? What would change if you demanded certainty (ilm qat'i) in all creedal issues instead of settling for predominant opinion?
?
The passage exposes the futility of clinging to weak evidence to support a comfortable position. Is there an argument you hold onto that you know deep down is weak? Will you release it and seek the truth with integrity, even if it is uncomfortable?

The Linguistic Reality of 'Khalq' as 'Makhluq'
Linguistic proof that 'khalq' directly means 'makhluq'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic argument that the word 'khalq' in Arabic originally means the created thing (makhluq), not merely the act of creation, and why this refutes the Ash'ari claim.

Core Idea
'Khalq' in Arabic is not a masdar but a noun for the created.

After establishing the methodological point about certainty, Imam al-Nasafi delivers a decisive linguistic blow. He argues that the word 'khalq' in the Arabic language is originally a noun meaning 'the created thing,' not a verbal noun used metaphorically. He appeals to the consensus of Arabic scholars and provides an analogy with 'wujud' and 'mawjud', which are synonymous.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim: 'khalq' means makhluq
Al-Nasafi asserts that the intended meaning of the verses is the created thing (makhluq), not the act of creation. He argues that this is not even metaphorical, but a direct lexical meaning.
This is a stronger claim than the earlier methodological point.
2
Understand the evidence from Arabic language
He states that the word 'khalq' in the original language (asl al-lugha) is a place/term for the created thing. Using 'khalq' to mean 'makhluq' is not a case of masdar standing for maf'ul; it is a direct meaning. He appeals to the consensus of all experts in Arabic, who would cry out in denial against the opponent.
The authority of the Arabic language is invoked.
3
Analyze the analogy with wujud/mawjud
Al-Nasafi draws an analogy: just as 'wujud' (existence) and 'mawjud' (existent) are synonymous and refer to the same reality, similarly 'khalq' (creation) and 'makhluq' (created) are essentially the same. The verbal noun here refers to the essence of the created thing.
This analogy refutes the Ash'ari claim that 'khalq' is an attribute distinct from the created thing.
4
Refute the Ash'ari counter-argument
The opponent might say that even if 'khalq' is a masdar, it refers to an attribute of Allah, not the created thing. Al-Nasafi dismisses this, arguing that the masdar here necessarily refers to the created thing, as it is impossible for it to be an attribute separate from the created.
This completes the refutation of the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الشبه السمعية للأشعرية
ثم نشتغل بالجواب عن شبهة السمعية لنتفرغ للمحاجة العقلية فنقول : مما أنكرت على من يقول لك أن المراد بقوله : هَذَا خَلْقُ اللَّهِ فَأَرُونِي مَاذَا خَلَقَ الَّذِينَ مِن دُونِهِ وغيره من الآيات، هذا مخلوق الله إذ لا وجه لك إلى إنكار جواز إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول في اللغة، كما في العلم والقدرة إذ هما يذكران ويراد بهما ما يتعلقان به من المعلوم والمقدور؟ فإن قلت : هذا مجازا فلا يصار إليه إلا بعد على قولك وهو مذهب الأشعري قيام الدليل قيل: إن للمجاز لا يكون موجبا للعلم قطعا ويقينا، وإن لم يقم بعد دليل المجاز لجواز أن يقوم، ولهذا نقول بالوقف في الآية العامة وإن لم يقارنها دليل التخصيص لما أن الاحتمال ينافي العلم ، فأما إذا كان إحدى الجهتين أرجح من الأخرى فقد يوجب علم الرأي فيجوز الاحتجاج به في مسائل الأحكام إذ هي تثبت بغالب الرأي، فأما في المسائل الاعتقادية التي لا وجه إلى القول بثبوتها إلا بإقامة دليل موجب للعمل قطعا فلا وجه إلى التعلق به وهو مذهب شيخنا أبي منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله
فلا وجه لك إذا إلى التعلق بهذه الآيات المحتملة للمجاز في محل النزاع، إلا إذا أقمت الدلالة على نفي احتمال المجاز، ولا دلالة على ذلك معك فإذا قلت إن الدلائل المعقولة تدل عليه فقد تركت الاحتجاج بالآية واشتغلت بالمعقول، وإن صح دليلك فقد وقعت لك الغنية عن التعلق بهذه الآيات، وإن لم يصح بطل احتجاجك بالآيات فكيفما دارت القصة ظهر سفهك في التعلق بهذه الآيات.
حمل الكلام على المجاز جائز بالدليل
على أنا نقيم الدلالة على أن المراد من الآيات هو المخلوق دون الخلق بطريق المجاز، وحمل الكلام على المجاز جائز بالدليل فمن الدلائل ما أقمنا من الدلالة السمعية التي لا مجال للشبهة في إبطال الاحتجاج بها على ما سبق ذكره، ونقيم بعد هذا الدلائل العقلية - إن شاء الله تعالى على أن قولك: إن إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول مجاز فلا يصار إليه إلا بدليل كلام فاسد جدا لأنك إذا أنكرت أن لفظة الخلق ليست بمصدر والمخلوق ليس بمفعول، وذكر الخلق والمراد به المخلوق ليس بطريق إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول بل هو موضع في أصل اللغة للمخلوق. فقد نادى عليك جميع أهل اللغة بالإنكار، وظهر جهلك بلسان العرب ومواصفاتهم وموازينهم وصيغاتهم التي استعملوها لتفاهم ما في الضمائر بها، وأحد لا يمكنه إنكار هذا ، وطريق سلفك أنهم يقولون : إن الخلق إن كان في اللغة مصدرا إلا أنه ينبئ عن معنى ذلك المعنى راجع إلى غير المفعول، وأنه ليس بمعنى وراءه كالوجود مع الموجود سواء ، فمن حقك أن تقول ذكر الخلق وأراد به المصدر إلا أن المصدر ها هنا راجع إلى ذات المخلوق، لاستحالة أن يكون غيره، وخصمك يقول : إن المصدر ها هنا صفة الله تعالى قائمة به والمخلوق ما يتعلق بتلك
The refutation of the sam'iyyah objections of the Ash'aris. Then we occupy ourselves with the answer to the sam'iyyah objection to free ourselves for rational argument, and we say: What do you deny from one who says to you that the intended meaning of His statement 'This is the creation (khalq) of Allah, so show me what those besides Him have created' and other verses is 'this is the created thing (makhluq) of Allah'? For you have no basis to deny the permissibility of placing the verbal noun (masdar) in the position of the object (maf'ul) in language, as in 'knowledge' (al-ilm) and 'power' (al-qudra), since these two are mentioned and what is intended by them is that to which they relate, i.e., the known and the empowered. If you say, 'This is a metaphorical usage (majaz), so it is not resorted to except with (the establishment of) evidence (dalil),' according to your statement, which is the doctrine of al-Ash'ari, it is said: Indeed, the metaphorical usage does not necessitate certain and definitive knowledge (al-ilm qat'an wa yaqinan), even if the evidence for the metaphor has not been established, because it is possible that it might be established. For this reason we say that there is suspension (waqf) regarding the general verse even if it is not accompanied by evidence of specification (takhsis), because probability (ihtimal) negates knowledge. As for when one of the two aspects is more preponderant (arjah) than the other, it may necessitate opinion-based knowledge (ilm al-ray'), and it is permissible to use it as evidence in matters of rulings (ahkam), since these are established by predominant opinion (ghalib al-ray). As for matters of belief (masa'il i'tiqadiyya), which have no basis for being established except by adducing evidence that necessitates certainty (qat'an), there is no basis for relying on it. This is the doctrine of our teacher Abu Mansur al-Maturidi - may Allah have mercy on him. So you have no basis to rely on these verses that are susceptible to metaphorical interpretation in the point of dispute, unless you adduce evidence negating the possibility of metaphorical interpretation, and you have no such evidence. If you say that rational proofs indicate it, then you have abandoned using the verse as evidence and engaged in rational argument. If your proof is sound, then you have no need to rely on these verses; and if it is unsound, then your argument from the verses is invalid. However the matter turns, your foolishness in relying on these verses becomes apparent. Carrying the speech to metaphorical interpretation is permissible with evidence. Moreover, we will adduce evidence that the intended meaning of the verses is the created thing (makhluq) and not 'creation' (khalq) via metaphorical interpretation (majaz), and carrying speech to metaphorical interpretation is permissible with evidence. Among the evidences are the sam'iyyah evidences we have adduced, which leave no room for doubt in invalidating the argument by means of them, as has been mentioned earlier. And after this we will adduce rational proofs - if Allah wills - that your statement: 'Indeed, placing the verbal noun in the position of the object is metaphorical, so it is not resorted to except with evidence' is utterly corrupt speech. Because if you deny that the word 'khalq' is a verbal noun and that 'makhluq' is an object, and that mentioning 'khalq' while intending 'makhluq' is not by way of placing the verbal noun in the position of the object, but rather it is a place in the original language for the created thing, then all experts in language would cry out in denial against you, and your ignorance of the language of the Arabs, their idioms, their standards, and their morphological patterns that they used to communicate what is in the minds becomes apparent. No one can deny this. And the way of your predecessors is that they say: Although 'khalq' in language is a verbal noun, it indicates a meaning that refers to something other than the object, and it is not a meaning behind it, like existence (wujud) and the existent (mawjud) are the same. So it is your right to say that he mentioned 'khalq' and intended by it the verbal noun, except that the verbal noun here refers to the essence of the created thing (dhat al-makhluq), due to the impossibility that it be other than that. And your opponent says: The verbal noun here is an attribute of Allah Most High subsisting in Him, and the created thing is that which relates to that attribute.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari school's sam'iyyah (scriptural) objections regarding the interpretation of the word 'khalq' in the Quran. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, presents two main arguments. First, he addresses the claim that verses like 'This is the creation of Allah' can be interpreted as 'this is the created thing of Allah' by way of placing the verbal noun (masdar) in the position of the object (maf'ul). He notes that the Ash'ari opponent accepts this linguistic possibility but argues that it is a metaphorical usage (majaz) that requires evidence. Al-Nasafi responds that metaphorical usage does not produce certain knowledge (al-ilm al-qat'i), and that in matters of belief (masa'il i'tiqadiyya) only certain evidence is acceptable, unlike in legal rulings where predominant opinion suffices. Thus, the opponent cannot rely on such verses without eliminating the possibility of metaphorical interpretation. The author then concludes that the opponent's position is self-defeating: if rational evidence is adduced, the verses become superfluous; if not, the argument fails. Second, al-Nasafi introduces a stronger linguistic argument: the word 'khalq' in the Arabic language is originally a noun meaning 'the created thing' (makhluq), not a verbal noun used metaphorically. He argues that the opponent's claim that using 'khalq' to mean 'makhluq' is a metaphor is false because in the original language it is a direct lexical term for the created thing. He supports this by appealing to the consensus of Arabic scholars and by analogy with terms like 'wujud' and 'mawjud', which are synonymous. The author further explains that even if 'khalq' is considered a verbal noun, it necessarily refers to the created thing because it is impossible for it to refer to something other than that. The opponent's counter-claim that 'khalq' is an eternal divine attribute is briefly mentioned but not refuted in the given text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

This lesson forces you to see that 'khalq' points directly to the concrete reality around you. Will you start seeing every created thing as a direct manifestation of Allah's attribute of creation, inseparable from the thing itself? Take a moment to look around and say to yourself: 'This is khalq, a created thing from Allah.'

Rational Permissibility and Scriptural Necessity of Seeing God
Introduces the topic of seeing God, stating the Ahl al-Haqq position on rational possibility and scriptural obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Sunni position (Ahl al-Haqq) holds that seeing Allah is rationally possible and scripturally obligatory, with believers seeing Him in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is both rationally possible and scripturally promised — a dual foundation.

This passage introduces a key topic in Islamic theology: the vision of Allah. The author states the position of Ahl al-Haqq, which combines rational argument and scriptural evidence. Before reading, note that this doctrine affirms both rational possibility (ja'iz) and scriptural obligation (wajib) for believers in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own words introduce the topic.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two distinct claims: (1) Seeing Allah is rationally permissible and known by rational evidence. (2) Believers will see Him in the afterlife, established by scriptural evidence. Notice the dual foundation.
3
Reflect on the dual foundation
Ask yourself why the author emphasizes both rational and scriptural evidence. What does this say about the Sunni methodology? The commentary will clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في جواز رؤية الله تعالى في العقل ووجوبها بالسمع: اختلف الناس في جواز رؤية الله تعالى. قال أهل الحق: إن الله تعالى جائز الرؤية يعرف ذلك بالدليل العقلي، ويراه المؤمنون في الآخرة بعد دخولهم الجنة. يثبت ذلك بالدلائل السمعية.
Discussion on the permissibility of seeing Allah, exalted, in reason and its obligatoriness by scripture: The people differed regarding the permissibility of seeing Allah, exalted. The People of Truth said: Indeed Allah, exalted, is permissible to see; that is known by rational evidence, and the believers will see Him in the afterlife after their entry into Paradise. That is established by scriptural evidences.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to a new topic: the vision of God (ru'yat Allah). It first states the subject: the rational permissibility and scriptural obligatoriness of seeing God. Then it reports that people have disagreed on whether seeing God is permissible. The author presents the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., the Sunnis): it is rationally permissible (ja'iz) to see God, and this is known by rational evidence; additionally, believers will see God in the afterlife after entering Paradise, and this is established by scriptural evidences (dalai'il sam'iyya). The passage thus outlines the dual foundation of the Sunni doctrine: rational possibility and scriptural affirmation.

Key Takeaway

The Linguistic and Rational Definition of Vision
Imam Nasafi argues against the opponents' concept of vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi argues that the term 'vision' cannot be applied to God in the same way it is applied to physical objects.

Core Idea
Vision of the Unseen cannot depart from the rational definition of vision.

Imam Nasafi tackles the term 'vision' (ru'ya) used by some theological groups to describe seeing God. He points out that their understanding does not match the linguistic and rational meaning of vision. By examining the conditions of physical vision, he shows that applying it to God is unintelligible. This lesson will walk through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between physical conditions and the divine.
2
Identify the conditions of physical vision
The passage lists several conditions that define vision in the physical world: facing, alignment, connection of rays, turning the eye, etc. Identify each one.
These conditions are all inapplicable to God.
3
Understand the analogy with knowledge
Nasafi draws an analogy: just as knowledge of the Unseen follows the same categories (necessary/acquired) as knowledge of the witnessed, so too must vision of the Unseen follow the rational definition of vision.
This shows that the opponents' claim is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الكلام من حيث نفس الرؤية فإنهم يقولون : إن ما تدعون في حق الصانع لن يعرف رؤية عند أهل اللسان، وفي المعارف بين أرباب العقول فإن رؤية ما ليس بجسم ولا عرض ولا مقابلة بين الرائي وبينه، ولا محاذاة ولا اتصال شعاع ولا بتقليب المقلة، وتوجيه الوجه إليه والإقبال عليه، واستدراك قدره، والوقوف على كله أو بعضه، لن يعرف رؤية، فإذا ما يقولون كلام يقال وليس لفحواه في الأفهام ولا لمعناه في الأوهام وتصوره محال ، وما يثبت في حق الغائب ينبغي أن يكون في حد ما يثبت في حق الشاهد ألا ترى أن العلم منا المتعلق بمعلوم شاهد لما لم يخرج من أن يكون ضروريا أو مكتسبا، فكان العلم منا بالغائب كذلك حتى كان العلم بالغائب عندكم وعند أكثرنا استدلاليا وعند بعض رؤسائنا ضروريا، فكذا الرؤية ينبغي أن لا تخرج في حق الغائب عن المتعارف المعقول. وبهذا التحقيق يتبين أن ما تزعمون أنه رؤية ليس برؤية وكلامكم في ذلك غير معقول.
As for the discussion from the perspective of the essence of vision: they say that what you claim regarding the Creator will not be known as vision among the people of language, nor in the cognitions of the people of intellect. For the vision of something that is neither a body nor an accident, nor is there facing between the seer and it, nor alignment, nor connection of rays, nor by turning the eye, nor directing the face toward it, nor approaching it, nor grasping its size, nor pausing over all or part of it—such will not be known as vision. So what they say is speech that is uttered, but its import has no place in minds nor its meaning in thoughts, and its conceptualization is impossible. And what is established for the Unseen should be within the limit of what is established for the witnessed. Do you not see that our knowledge pertaining to a witnessed object, since it does not go out of being either necessary or acquired, so our knowledge of the Unseen is likewise—until knowledge of the Unseen according to you and most of us is inferential, and according to some of our leaders is necessary. So likewise vision should not, regarding the Unseen, depart from what is customary and rational. And by this verification it becomes clear that what you claim is vision is not vision, and your speech concerning that is unintelligible.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual analysis of the term 'vision' (ru'ya). The author argues that what the opponents claim as vision of God does not match the conventional meaning of vision in language and reason. Vision in the physical world involves conditions like facing, alignment, connection of rays, etc. Since these are inapplicable to God, the term 'vision' cannot be applied in the same sense. He further uses an analogy with knowledge: just as knowledge of the Unseen does not depart from the categories of necessary or acquired knowledge, so vision of the Unseen should not depart from the rational and customary understanding of vision. Thus the claim is unintelligible.

Key Terms

أهل اللسان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision of God can be understood in the same way as physical vision.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the term 'vision' when applied to God must be understood differently; physical conditions like facing, alignment, and connection of rays do not apply.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point in this passage?
A) God cannot be seen at all
B) The opponents' understanding of vision does not match the linguistic and rational definition
C) Vision of God requires special conditions
D) The term 'vision' is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the opponents' claim of vision is not true vision because it does not fit the rational definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Nasafi says that the opponents' speech about vision is 'uttered but has no place in minds.' Is your own talk about God sometimes empty—words without real understanding? How can you commit to ensuring that every statement you make about the Unseen is rooted in sound reasoning and revelation?
?
The lesson argues that our understanding of the Unseen must follow the same rational categories as the witnessed. Do you approach your knowledge of God with the same humility and discipline as you do physical realities? Or do you claim certainty where you only have assumption? What step will you take today to align your convictions with truth?

The Contradiction of Temporary Vision in Paradise
Al-Nasafi presents the opponents' argument that temporary vision would cause suffering, contradicting Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponents' claim that vision of Allah occurs temporarily in the afterlife leads to a contradiction with the nature of Paradise, using the argument from pleasure and suffering.

Core Idea
If the greatest pleasure ends, it brings suffering—but Paradise has no suffering.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents a key argument used by some theologians against the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter. The argument focuses on the nature of reward and pleasure: if seeing Allah is the greatest blessing, then its cessation would cause pain, which is incompatible with the serene bliss of Paradise. By following this logical chain, you will see why the opponents' position is considered internally contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the repeated phrases 'لا شك' indicating certainty.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument has three main premises: vision is the greatest pleasure, it is temporary, and its cessation causes suffering. Find these in the text.
The author uses these to conclude that vision cannot be in Paradise.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that vision must not occur in the Hereafter because Paradise is free from suffering. Consider why this contradicts the opponents' claim.
This argument is presented as a doubt (شبهة) that the author will later refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام من حيث الاستدلال بصفة الرؤية والمكان الذي تدعون فيه الرؤية، فهو أن الرؤية تحصل عندكم إكراما للعباد بطريق الثواب ويتلذذ بها العبد لا محالة، والتلذذ بالنعم يكون مقدارها، فمن كان أعظم خطرا كان أعظم لذة، ولا شك أن رؤية الله تعالى أعظم النعم فتكون أعظمها لذة، ثم الإكرام بالرؤية عندكم لا يكون على الدوام بل يوجد ثم ينعدم، ولا شك أن الرؤية إذا انعدمت انعدمت اللذة الحاصلة بها، ولا شك أن العبد يتأذى بزوال أعلى اللذات إلى أدناها ويتنغص عليه أدنى النعم التي هو فيها عند زوال أعلاها، والجنة ليست بدار يتأذى فيها ولي الله تعالى أو تتنغص عليه النعم فيها وهذا مما يوجب أن لا يرى الله تعالى في الآخرة. فهذه هي الشبه المشهورة للخصوم ونستعين الله تعالى على حلها ودفعها على وجه لا يبقى للسامع فيها شك ولا شبهة فإنه لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله.
As for the discussion regarding the inference from the attribute of vision and the place in which you claim vision, it is that vision occurs according to you as an honor for the servants by way of reward, and the servant inevitably takes pleasure in it. And pleasure in blessings is proportionate to their magnitude. So whoever is of greater significance has greater pleasure. There is no doubt that seeing Allah the Exalted is the greatest of blessings, so it is the greatest in pleasure. Then the honor with vision according to you is not perpetual; rather it exists then ceases. There is no doubt that when vision ceases, the pleasure resulting from it ceases. There is no doubt that the servant suffers from the removal of the highest pleasure to the lowest, and the lowest blessings he is in become distasteful to him when the highest is removed. And Paradise is not a place where the friend of Allah suffers or where blessings become distasteful to him. This necessitates that Allah is not seen in the Hereafter. These are the famous doubts of the opponents, and we seek Allah's help to solve and refute them in a manner that leaves no doubt or ambiguity for the listener, for there is no power or strength except with Allah.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the nature of reward and pleasure. The author argues that if vision is the greatest pleasure, then its cessation would cause suffering, but Paradise has no suffering. Since the opponents claim vision occurs then ceases, that would entail suffering, which is incompatible with Paradise. Therefore, vision cannot occur in the Hereafter. The author concludes by noting these are the opponents' famous doubts, and he will refute them.

Misconception: Temporary vision in Paradise is a net positive without pain.
Correction: Cessation of the greatest pleasure inevitably causes suffering, which is incompatible with Paradise.

A) Because vision is impossible
B) Because the pleasure of vision would cease, causing suffering, which is not in Paradise
C) Because God cannot be seen
D) Because the servants are not worthy

Key Takeaway
The opponents' claim fails because temporary vision would lead to suffering, contradicting the peace of Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Mu'tazila Objection: Cessation of Vision Causes Harm
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila argument that loss of ru'ya harms the believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that the cessation of ru'ya would cause harm incompatible with Paradise, and why this is presented as a doubt requiring refutation.

Core Idea
Loss of the highest delight makes lower blessings distasteful — but Paradise is no place for harm.

The Mu'tazila raise a subtle objection: if ru'ya is the greatest delight, its cessation would make Paradise's bliss imperfect. Nasafi presents this argument in full before promising to refute it. This lesson walks through the reasoning and sets up the Ahl al-Haqq response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the logical flow from premises to conclusion.
The passage ends with a promise to rebut, so it is a presentation of the opponent's view.
2
Identify the key premises
List the steps: ru'ya is the greatest delight; it is not perpetual; its cessation ends the delight; loss of highest delight makes lower ones distasteful; Paradise has no distaste.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that Allah cannot be seen in the Hereafter because it would cause harm in Paradise. Notice the logical force.
This is a reductio ad absurdum based on the nature of Paradise.
4
Reflect on the label 'doubt'
Nasafi calls this a 'shubha' (doubt). Ask yourself why it is not considered a definitive proof. This sets up the need for refutation.
The Ahl al-Haqq hold that ru'ya is perpetual, so the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[العبد يتأذى بزوال الرؤية]: وأما الكلام من حيث الاستدلال بصفة الرؤية والمكان الذي تدعون فيه الرؤية، فهو أن الرؤية تحصل عندكم إكراما للعباد بطريق الثواب ويتلذذ بها العبد لا محالة، والتلذذ بالنعم يكون مقدارها، فمن كان أعظم خطرا كان أعظم لذة، ولا شك أن رؤية الله تعالى أعظم النعم فتكون أعظمها لذة، ثم الإكرام بالرؤية عندكم لا يكون على الدوام بل يوجد ثم ينعدم، ولا شك أن الرؤية إذا انعدمت انعدمت اللذة الحاصلة بها، ولا شك أن العبد يتأذى بزوال أعلى اللذات إلى أدناها ويتنغص عليه أدنى النعم التي هو فيها عند زوال أعلاها، والجنة ليست بدار يتأذى فيها ولي الله تعالى أو تتنغص عليه النعم فيها وهذا مما يوجب أن لا يرى الله تعالى في الآخرة. فهذه هي الشبه المشهورة للخصوم ونستعين الله تعالى على حلها ودفعها على وجه لا يبقى للسامع فيها شك ولا شبهة فإنه لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله.
[The servant is harmed by the cessation of vision]: As for the discourse concerning the argument by the attribute of vision and the place where you claim vision, it is that vision occurs, according to you, as an honor for the servants by way of reward, and the servant necessarily delights in it. And delight in blessings is proportionate to their magnitude; whoever is greater in rank has greater delight. There is no doubt that the vision of Allah the Exalted is the greatest of blessings, so it is the greatest in delight. Then, the honor of vision with you is not perpetual, but occurs and then ceases. There is no doubt that when vision ceases, the delight resulting from it ceases. And there is no doubt that the servant is harmed by the loss of the highest delights to lower ones, and the lowest blessings he is in become distasteful when the highest ceases. Paradise is not a place where a friend of Allah is harmed or blessings become distasteful to him. This necessitates that Allah the Exalted is not seen in the Hereafter. These are the famous doubts of the opponents, and we seek help from Allah to solve and rebut them in a way that leaves no doubt or uncertainty for the listener, for there is no power or strength except through Allah.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage presents a famous objection of the Mu'tazila against the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the Hereafter. The argument is structured as a reductio ad absurdum based on the nature of Paradise and the concept of reward. The author states the premise accepted by the Ahl al-Haqq: that the vision of Allah is a great blessing granted as an honor and reward, and that the believer delights in it proportionally to its greatness. He then argues that, according to the Mu'tazila, this vision is not permanent; it occurs and then ceases. Since the cessation of vision eliminates the associated delight, the believer would be harmed by moving from the highest delight to lower ones, and the remaining lesser blessings would become distasteful. But Paradise is not a place of harm or distaste for Allah's friends. Therefore, the Mu'tazila conclude that Allah cannot be seen in the Hereafter. The author ends by acknowledging this as a doubt (shubha) and expresses reliance on Allah to refute it.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الإكرام
الثواب
التلذذ
النعم
الآخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that vision of Allah itself is impossible.
Correction: The objection does not deny the possibility of ru'ya; it argues that its cessation would cause harm in Paradise, which is unacceptable.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument in this passage, why would the believer be harmed by the cessation of ru'ya?
A) Because the believer would no longer see Allah and would be deprived of a greater reward.
B) Because the loss of the highest delight makes even lower blessings distasteful.
C) Because Paradise is a place of perpetual vision without interruption.
D) Because the vision of Allah is not a true delight.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if ru'ya ceases, the believer suffers harm inconsistent with Paradise, so ru'ya cannot occur — a doubt Nasafi will refute.

?

But for you, living in this world, do you treat the thought of Allah's pleasure as a temporary feeling or as the ultimate goal that should never be compromised? What would it cost you to make seeking His vision your highest aim?
?
Imagine standing in Paradise and realizing that you could have had the vision of Allah but lost it because you were not bold enough in your faith. Does that possibility scare you enough today to surrender your doubts and trust in Allah's promise?

First Proof: Moses' Belief in the Visibility of Allah
First of three Quranic proofs for ru'ya from Musa's story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Moses' request to see Allah demonstrates that vision of Allah is possible, and why attributing ignorance to prophets is impossible.

Core Idea
Moses would not have asked to see Allah if it were impossible.

This lesson introduces the first of three arguments from the story of Moses used by the Ahl al-Haqq to prove the possibility of seeing Allah. Nasafi points out that Moses' very request implies his belief in the possibility, as prophets cannot be ignorant of their Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it begins by citing the Quranic verse and then presents the first aspect of the argument.
The argument relies on the infallibility of prophets.
2
Understand the logic
The argument goes: Moses asked to see Allah → Moses believed it was possible → If it were impossible, Moses would be ignorant → Prophets cannot be ignorant → Therefore, it is possible. Trace this chain.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is powerful against those who deny vision. What would they have to say about Moses?
This shows the importance of prophetic infallibility in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أهل الحق فإنهم احتجوا بقول الله تعالى خبرًا عن موسى عليه السلام رب أرني أنظر إليك الآية. والاستدلال بالآية من وجوه: أحدها: أن موسى عليه السلام اعتقد أن الله تعالى مرئي، ولو لم يكن مرئيا كان هذا منه جهلا بخالقه ونسبة الأنبياء إلى الجهل بالله كفر. الثاني: أنه تعالى قال لن ترين نفى رؤية موسى إياه وما أخبر أن ذاته ليس بمرئي فإنه ما قال لست بمرئي بل قال لن ترين ولو لم تكن ذاته مرئيا لما أخبر أنه ليس بمرئي، إذ الحالة كانت حالة الحاجة إلى البيان لأنه على زعم هؤلاء الملحدين جهل الله تعالى والحكمة تقتضي البيان عند الحاجة إلى البيان. والثالث: أنه تعالى قال ولكن انظر إلى الجبل فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تريني فالله تعالى علق الرؤية باستقرار الجبل واستقرار الجبل من الجائزات، والأصل أن تعليق الفعل بما هو جائز الوجود يدل على جوازه، وتعليقه بما هو ممتنع الوجود يدل على امتناعه وعدم تكونه، وتعليقه بما هو متحقق الوجود تحقيق له، وها هنا جائز الوجود وهو استقرار الجبل، ودليل جواز وجوده قوله تعالى: فلما تجلى ربه للجبل جعله دكا أخبر الله تعالى أنه جعله دكا لا أنه اندك بنفسه، وما أوجده الله تعالى كان جائزا أن لا يوجد لو لم يوجده الله تعالى، إذ الله تعالى مختار
As for the People of Truth, they argued with the saying of Allah the Exalted, reporting from Moses, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' [7:143]. And the argument from the verse has several aspects: First, that Moses, peace be upon him, believed that Allah the Exalted is visible, and if He were not visible, this would have been ignorance from him of his Creator, and attributing ignorance to the prophets regarding Allah is disbelief. Second, that He the Exalted said, 'You will not see Me' [7:143]; He negated Moses' seeing Him but did not inform that His essence is not visible, for He did not say 'I am not visible' but said 'You will not see Me'. And if His essence were not visible, He would have informed that it is not visible, for the situation was a situation of need for clarification, because according to the claim of these atheists, [Moses] was ignorant of Allah, and wisdom necessitates clarification when there is need for clarification. Third, that He the Exalted said, 'But look at the mountain; if it should remain in place, then you will see Me' [7:143]. So Allah the Exalted conditioned vision on the stability of the mountain, and the stability of the mountain is among possible things. The principle is that conditioning an action on what is possible in existence indicates its permissibility, conditioning it on what is impossible indicates its impossibility and non-occurrence, and conditioning it on what is actualized indicates its realization. Here it is a possible existence, that is, the stability of the mountain. The proof of the permissibility of its existence is His saying, the Exalted: 'When his Lord appeared to the mountain, He made it level' [7:143]; Allah the Exalted informed that He made it level, not that it collapsed by itself. And what Allah the Exalted brought into existence was possible to not exist had Allah not brought it into existence, for Allah the Exalted is free of choice.
Source pages p. 598

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of the Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth) for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the Hereafter, based on the Quranic verse about Prophet Moses (peace be upon him). The author states that the Ahl al-Haqq argue using Allah's words reporting Moses' request: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You.' Then he enumerates three aspects (wujuh) of the argument. First, Moses' belief that Allah is visible: if Allah were not inherently visible, Moses would have been ignorant of his Creator, and attributing ignorance to prophets regarding Allah is disbelief (kufr). Therefore, Moses' request proves the possibility of vision. Second, Allah's response 'You will not see Me' negates Moses' actual seeing but does not negate the possibility of vision in itself. Allah did not say 'I am not visible' but said 'You will not see Me.' If the essence were not visible, Allah would have clearly stated so, especially since the situation required clarification, as Moses—according to the opponents—was supposedly ignorant. Wisdom demands clarification when needed. Third, Allah conditioned the vision on the mountain's stability: 'But look at the mountain; if it should remain in place, then you will see Me.' The mountain's stability is among possible things (ja'izat). The author lays out a principle: conditioning an action on what is possible indicates its permissibility; conditioning on what is impossible indicates impossibility; conditioning on what is actual indicates actualization. Here the condition is possible, as shown by the fact that Allah 'made it level' (ja'alahu dakkan) by His own act, not that it collapsed by itself. Since Allah brought it about, its existence is contingent on His will, proving that the condition (stability) is possible. Thus, the vision it conditions is also possible. The passage ends by emphasizing Allah's free choice (mukhtar).

Key Terms
الرؤية
موسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Moses' request shows he actually saw Allah, but the first proof only establishes possibility, not actuality.
Correction: The argument from Moses' request only proves that vision is possible; it does not claim that Moses saw Allah at that moment. The second and third aspects further clarify.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the first proof from the story of Moses?
A) Moses actually saw Allah.
B) Moses believed it was possible to see Allah, and prophets cannot be ignorant.
C) Allah showed Himself to the mountain.
D) The mountain's stability proves vision is possible.

Key Takeaway
Moses' request to see Allah proves that vision of Allah is possible, because prophets cannot be ignorant of their Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have the same level of certainty as Prophet Musa that Allah is real and that you will see Him in the Hereafter? If not, what concrete step will you take today to strengthen that belief?
?
When you reflect on your own doubts or lack of clarity about Allah’s attributes, does that reveal a gap between your knowledge and the perfect knowledge of the prophets? How can you close that gap starting now?
?
If someone asked you tomorrow why you believe you can see Allah in Paradise, would you be able to give a clear answer based on the Quran and the example of the prophets? What will you do to prepare that answer today?

Second Proof: The Negation 'You Will Not See Me'
Second proof: linguistic analysis of Allah's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's response 'You will not see Me' does not negate the possibility of vision, but only temporary permission.

Core Idea
Allah said 'You will not see Me,' not 'I am not visible.'

The second argument from the verse focuses on Allah's reply. Nasafi explains that Allah did not say 'I am not visible' but only denied Moses' seeing at that moment. If vision were inherently impossible, Allah would have made that explicit, especially when clarification was needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the distinction between negating actual seeing and negating possibility.
2
Analyze the wording
Compare 'lan tarani' (you will not see Me) with 'lastu bi-mar'iy' (I am not visible). Why did Allah choose the former?
If vision were impossible, 'lastu bi-mar'iy' would have been more direct.
3
Understand the context
The situation required clarification because of the claim that Moses was ignorant. Allah's wisdom demands clear statement when needed.
This strengthens the argument that vision is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أهل الحق فإنهم احتجوا بقول الله تعالى خبرًا عن موسى عليه السلام رب أرني أنظر إليك الآية. والاستدلال بالآية من وجوه: أحدها: أن موسى عليه السلام اعتقد أن الله تعالى مرئي، ولو لم يكن مرئيا كان هذا منه جهلا بخالقه ونسبة الأنبياء إلى الجهل بالله كفر. الثاني: أنه تعالى قال لن ترين نفى رؤية موسى إياه وما أخبر أن ذاته ليس بمرئي فإنه ما قال لست بمرئي بل قال لن ترين ولو لم تكن ذاته مرئيا لما أخبر أنه ليس بمرئي، إذ الحالة كانت حالة الحاجة إلى البيان لأنه على زعم هؤلاء الملحدين جهل الله تعالى والحكمة تقتضي البيان عند الحاجة إلى البيان. والثالث: أنه تعالى قال ولكن انظر إلى الجبل فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تريني فالله تعالى علق الرؤية باستقرار الجبل واستقرار الجبل من الجائزات، والأصل أن تعليق الفعل بما هو جائز الوجود يدل على جوازه، وتعليقه بما هو ممتنع الوجود يدل على امتناعه وعدم تكونه، وتعليقه بما هو متحقق الوجود تحقيق له، وها هنا جائز الوجود وهو استقرار الجبل، ودليل جواز وجوده قوله تعالى: فلما تجلى ربه للجبل جعله دكا أخبر الله تعالى أنه جعله دكا لا أنه اندك بنفسه، وما أوجده الله تعالى كان جائزا أن لا يوجد لو لم يوجده الله تعالى، إذ الله تعالى مختار
As for the People of Truth, they argued with the saying of Allah the Exalted, reporting from Moses, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' [7:143]. And the argument from the verse has several aspects: First, that Moses, peace be upon him, believed that Allah the Exalted is visible, and if He were not visible, this would have been ignorance from him of his Creator, and attributing ignorance to the prophets regarding Allah is disbelief. Second, that He the Exalted said, 'You will not see Me' [7:143]; He negated Moses' seeing Him but did not inform that His essence is not visible, for He did not say 'I am not visible' but said 'You will not see Me'. And if His essence were not visible, He would have informed that it is not visible, for the situation was a situation of need for clarification, because according to the claim of these atheists, [Moses] was ignorant of Allah, and wisdom necessitates clarification when there is need for clarification. Third, that He the Exalted said, 'But look at the mountain; if it should remain in place, then you will see Me' [7:143]. So Allah the Exalted conditioned vision on the stability of the mountain, and the stability of the mountain is among possible things. The principle is that conditioning an action on what is possible in existence indicates its permissibility, conditioning it on what is impossible indicates its impossibility and non-occurrence, and conditioning it on what is actualized indicates its realization. Here it is a possible existence, that is, the stability of the mountain. The proof of the permissibility of its existence is His saying, the Exalted: 'When his Lord appeared to the mountain, He made it level' [7:143]; Allah the Exalted informed that He made it level, not that it collapsed by itself. And what Allah the Exalted brought into existence was possible to not exist had Allah not brought it into existence, for Allah the Exalted is free of choice.
Source pages p. 598

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of the Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth) for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the Hereafter, based on the Quranic verse about Prophet Moses (peace be upon him). The author states that the Ahl al-Haqq argue using Allah's words reporting Moses' request: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You.' Then he enumerates three aspects (wujuh) of the argument. First, Moses' belief that Allah is visible: if Allah were not inherently visible, Moses would have been ignorant of his Creator, and attributing ignorance to prophets regarding Allah is disbelief (kufr). Therefore, Moses' request proves the possibility of vision. Second, Allah's response 'You will not see Me' negates Moses' actual seeing but does not negate the possibility of vision in itself. Allah did not say 'I am not visible' but said 'You will not see Me.' If the essence were not visible, Allah would have clearly stated so, especially since the situation required clarification, as Moses—according to the opponents—was supposedly ignorant. Wisdom demands clarification when needed. Third, Allah conditioned the vision on the mountain's stability: 'But look at the mountain; if it should remain in place, then you will see Me.' The mountain's stability is among possible things (ja'izat). The author lays out a principle: conditioning an action on what is possible indicates its permissibility; conditioning on what is impossible indicates impossibility; conditioning on what is actual indicates actualization. Here the condition is possible, as shown by the fact that Allah 'made it level' (ja'alahu dakkan) by His own act, not that it collapsed by itself. Since Allah brought it about, its existence is contingent on His will, proving that the condition (stability) is possible. Thus, the vision it conditions is also possible. The passage ends by emphasizing Allah's free choice (mukhtar).

Key Terms
الرؤية
موسى
البيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'lan tarani' means vision is absolutely impossible.
Correction: 'Lan tarani' only denies that Moses would see at that time; it does not deny the possibility of vision itself.

Checkpoint
What is the key linguistic point in the second proof?
A) Allah said 'I am not visible'.
B) Allah said 'You will not see Me' instead of 'I am not visible'.
C) The mountain collapsed.
D) Moses was ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Allah's response 'You will not see Me' does not negate the possibility of vision; it only denies it at that moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Allah says 'You will not see Me' to Musa, do you feel a sense of distance or invitation? How does your heart respond to the idea that you may not see Allah in this life—does it increase your longing or your despair?
?
If you were in Musa's place and received a 'not now' from Allah, would your faith remain steadfast or would you question His wisdom? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's plan?
?
The proof hinges on Allah's precise wording. Have you ever considered that every word of Allah's response to you—even a 'no'—carries profound wisdom and mercy? How can you train yourself to look for the deeper meaning in His answers rather than focusing on the surface?

Third Proof: The Conditional with the Mountain
Third proof: logical argument from conditional statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the condition of the mountain's stability proves that vision of Allah is possible, based on the principle of conditioning on what is possible.

Core Idea
Conditioning vision on a possible event shows that vision itself is possible.

The third and most detailed argument uses Allah's statement: 'But look at the mountain; if it should remain in place, then you will see Me.' Nasafi explains the logical principle: conditioning an action on what is possible indicates the permissibility of that action. Since the mountain's stability is possible, the vision conditioned upon it is also possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the condition and the conditioned.
2
Understand the principle
Note the three-part rule: conditioning on possible indicates permissibility, on impossible indicates impossibility, on actual indicates realization.
This is a general logical principle in kalam.
3
Apply the principle
Here the condition (mountain's stability) is possible, as proven by the Quran where Allah made it level by His own act.
Thus vision is possible.
4
Reflect on Allah's freedom
The passage ends by emphasizing Allah's free choice, showing everything depends on His will.
This reinforces that nothing is inherently impossible if Allah wills.

Source Text

وأما أهل الحق فإنهم احتجوا بقول الله تعالى خبرًا عن موسى عليه السلام رب أرني أنظر إليك الآية. والاستدلال بالآية من وجوه: أحدها: أن موسى عليه السلام اعتقد أن الله تعالى مرئي، ولو لم يكن مرئيا كان هذا منه جهلا بخالقه ونسبة الأنبياء إلى الجهل بالله كفر. الثاني: أنه تعالى قال لن ترين نفى رؤية موسى إياه وما أخبر أن ذاته ليس بمرئي فإنه ما قال لست بمرئي بل قال لن ترين ولو لم تكن ذاته مرئيا لما أخبر أنه ليس بمرئي، إذ الحالة كانت حالة الحاجة إلى البيان لأنه على زعم هؤلاء الملحدين جهل الله تعالى والحكمة تقتضي البيان عند الحاجة إلى البيان. والثالث: أنه تعالى قال ولكن انظر إلى الجبل فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تريني فالله تعالى علق الرؤية باستقرار الجبل واستقرار الجبل من الجائزات، والأصل أن تعليق الفعل بما هو جائز الوجود يدل على جوازه، وتعليقه بما هو ممتنع الوجود يدل على امتناعه وعدم تكونه، وتعليقه بما هو متحقق الوجود تحقيق له، وها هنا جائز الوجود وهو استقرار الجبل، ودليل جواز وجوده قوله تعالى: فلما تجلى ربه للجبل جعله دكا أخبر الله تعالى أنه جعله دكا لا أنه اندك بنفسه، وما أوجده الله تعالى كان جائزا أن لا يوجد لو لم يوجده الله تعالى، إذ الله تعالى مختار

The Linguistic Rule: 'Naẓar' with 'ilā' Indicates Vision
The author states the linguistic rule and provides Quranic and poetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic rule that when the verb 'naẓara' is attached to the face and directed with the preposition 'ilā', it means vision, not waiting.

Core Idea
If 'naẓara' is paired with 'ilā', it's about seeing, not waiting.

This passage establishes a key linguistic principle: when 'naẓar' is attached to the face and directed with 'ilā', it cannot mean waiting. The author proves this by citing a Quranic verse and a line of poetry that use 'naẓar' without 'ilā' to indicate waiting, contrasting them with the verse in question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its literal translation carefully. Notice how the author states the rule and then provides evidence.
The passage contains a rule followed by two examples.
2
Identify the linguistic rule
Find the sentence where the author explicitly states that 'naẓar' attached to the face and directed with 'ilā' cannot mean waiting.
The rule is: 'al-naẓar al-muḍāf ilā al-wajh al-muʿaddā ilā al-manẓūr ilayhi bi-ḥarf ilā lan yakūn al-murād minhu naẓar al-intiẓār.'
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Examine the Quranic verse (27:35) where 'fānāẓiratun' is used without 'ilā' to indicate waiting. Contrast it with the verse in question that uses 'ilā rabbihā nāẓirah'.
The author uses this to show that waiting is expressed without 'ilā'.
4
Analyze the poetic evidence
Read the line of poetry where 'nāẓirah' is used without 'ilā' to mean waiting. This reinforces the rule.
The poet's line is about waiting for someone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: إن مراد الشاعر من هذا اليمامة سمي بيوم بكر لأن القتال كان يوم فيه مع بني حنيفة بن يخم رهط مسيلمة الكذاب، وبنو حنيفة بطن من بكر بن وائل فسمي ذلك اليوم يوم بكر لهذا، وعنى بالرحمن مسيلمة الكذاب فإنهم كانوا يسمونه رحمن اليمامة. وبهذا أبطلنا تأويل من قال: معناه ثواب ربها منتظرة لما مر أن النظر المضاف إلى الوجه المعدى إلى المنظور إليه بحرف إلى لن يكون المراد منه نظر الانتظار. فأما إذا أريد به الانتظار فإنه لا يعلق بالوجه ولا يعدى بإلى قال الله تعالى خبرا عن تلك المرأة: فَنَاظِرَةٌ بِمَ يَرْجِعُ الْمُرْسَلُونَ ولم تقيد بالوجه ولا عدي بكلمة إلى لما أريد به الانتظار. وقال الشاعر: فإن بك صدر هذا اليوم ولـى فإن غـ لنـاظره قريـ أي لمنتظرة، فأما قول جميل بن معمر: إني إليك لما وعدت لناظر نظر الذليل إلى العزيز القاهر فإن المراد منه النظر الذي هو رؤية العين لأنه عداه بكلمة إلى فمعناه: إني انظر إليك بالذل والانكسار لأن النظر إلى المأمول على هذا الوجه يبعثه على المسارعة إلى قضاء الحاجة وانجاز الوعد. وفي قول القائل: ويوم بذي قار رأيت وجوههم إلى الموت من وقع السيوف نواظر أراد أيضا نظر الرؤية لما عداه بكلمة إلى. ثم إن بعض الناس قالوا: المراد به حقيقة الموت، وهو عندنا مرئي إذ كل موجود جائز الرؤية على ما نبين – إن شاء الله تعالى - وبعضهم قالوا أراد بالموت أسباب الموت كالضرب والطعن وهي مرئية في العادة والشيء قد يسمى باسم ما يؤول إليه. وبعضهم قال: أراد بالموت الإبطال الذي يوجد الموت بسبب ضربهم وطعنهم وإقدامهم في الهيجاء كمال قال جرير: أنا الموت الذي خُبِّرت عنّ فليس لهارب مني نجا وقال آخر: يأيها الراكب المسجي مطيته سائل بني أسد مــا هــذا الـصـوت وقل لهم بادروا بالعذر والتمسوا قولا يقربكم إنـي أنــا المـوت. والذي يدل على أن حمل الآية على الانتظار فاسد وجهان من المعقول: أحدهما راجع إلى نفس المنتظر فهو أن الثواب يومئذ يكون موجودا لأن الجنة دار وقوع الثواب لا دار الانتظار لذلك موجود في الدنيا، والمعدوم هو الذي ينتظر لا الموجود، وما يقال في مبتذل الكلام انتظر زيدا معناه انتظر حضوره
He said: Indeed, the poet's intent from this al-Yamama was called 'Day of Bakr' because the fighting occurred on that day with Banu Hanifa, the clan of Musaylima the liar, and Banu Hanifa are a branch of Bakr ibn Wa'il, so that day was called 'Day of Bakr' for this reason. And by 'the Most Merciful' he meant Musaylima the liar, for they used to call him 'Rahman of al-Yamama'. And by this we have invalidated the interpretation of those who said: its meaning is 'awaiting the reward of her Lord', since it has passed that 'al-naẓar' attached to the face and directed to the object of sight with the letter 'ilā' cannot have the meaning of 'naẓar al-intiẓār' (the look of waiting). As for when waiting is intended, it is not attached to the face nor directed with 'ilā'. Allah the Exalted said, reporting about that woman: 'So she is watching (fānāẓiratun) with what the messengers return', and it was not restricted to the face nor directed with the word 'ilā' because waiting was intended. And the poet said: 'For if your chest of this day has turned away, then she is waiting (nāẓiruhu) for Qurya', meaning 'waiting'. As for the saying of Jamil ibn Ma'mar: 'I am, since you promised, looking (nāẓir) to you, the look of the lowly to the mighty omnipotent', the intent from it is the look that is vision of the eye because he directed it with the word 'ilā'. So its meaning: I look at you with lowliness and contrition, because looking at the hoped-for in this manner urges him to hasten to fulfill the need and fulfill the promise. And in the saying of the speaker: 'And on the day of Dhi Qār I saw their faces to death from the falling of swords looking'—he also intended the look of vision because he directed it with the word 'ilā'. Then some people said: the intended by it is the reality of death, and it is in our view visible, since every existent is permissible of vision as we will explain—if Allah wills. And some said: they intended by death the causes of death, like striking and stabbing, and they are visible in custom, and a thing may be named after that to which it leads. And some said: they intended by death the annihilation that occurs due to their striking, stabbing, and advancing in the fray, as Jarir said: 'I am death that you were informed of, so no one fleeing from me escapes.' And another said: 'O rider whose mount is journeying, ask the Banu Asad what this sound is, and tell them to hasten with excuse and seek a word that brings you near; indeed, I am death.' And what indicates that applying the verse to waiting is corrupt are two aspects from reason: one of them pertains to the one waiting himself—that reward on that day would exist, because Paradise is the abode of occurrence of reward, not the abode of waiting; therefore it exists in this world, and what is non-existent is what is waited for, not what exists. And what is said in common speech, 'I waited for Zayd', meaning 'I waited for his arrival'.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the interpretation that the word 'nāẓirāt' (in the verse 'toward their Lord looking') means waiting for reward rather than seeing. The author begins by explaining a line of poetry that mentions 'the day of Bakr' and 'the Most Merciful' referring to Musaylima the liar, to show that the word 'naẓar' can be used in contexts of vision. He then directly invalidates the claim that the verse means 'awaiting the reward of her Lord' by stating a linguistic rule: when 'naẓar' is attached to the face (al-wajh) and directed with the preposition 'ilā', it cannot mean waiting. To prove this, he cites a Quranic verse where 'fānāẓiratun' is used without 'ilā' to indicate waiting, and a line of poetry where 'nāẓirah' means waiting without 'ilā'. In contrast, the verse in question uses 'ilā rabbihā nāẓirah' (to their Lord looking), so it must mean seeing. He then examines a line by Jamil ibn Ma'mar that uses 'nāẓir' with 'ilā', arguing that it clearly means vision because it is directed with 'ilā', and the context of supplication reinforces this. Similarly, a line about faces looking to death with swords proves the same point. The author then discusses three interpretations of 'death' in such poetry: as the reality of death (which is visible because all existents are visible), as the causes of death (like striking, which are customarily visible), or as annihilation personified (as in Jarir's poetry). This shows that even metaphorical uses rely on the concept of visibility. Finally, the author introduces two rational proofs that the waiting interpretation is invalid. The first proof is that reward on the Day of Judgment already exists in Paradise; waiting is for something non-existent, but reward exists, so waiting makes no sense. The common phrase 'I waited for Zayd' means waiting for his arrival, not for Zayd himself who already exists. The second proof is cut off at the page break but is introduced as the second of two aspects.
