Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nāẓirāt' in the verse can mean waiting for reward.
Correction: The presence of 'ilā' and attachment to the face proves it means vision, not waiting, because waiting is expressed without 'ilā' as shown in the Quran and poetry.

Checkpoint
What linguistic evidence does the author use to prove that 'nāẓirāt' means seeing, not waiting?
A) The word 'nāẓirāt' is used in poetry to mean waiting.
B) The preposition 'ilā' is used with the verb 'naẓara' in the verse, whereas waiting is expressed without 'ilā'.
C) The verse says 'toward their Lord' which implies direction, so it must be waiting.
D) The author quotes a hadith about waiting.

Key Takeaway
When the verb 'naẓara' is used with the preposition 'ilā' and attached to the face, it necessarily means vision, not waiting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'ilā rabbihā nāẓirah', do you deeply long for the vision of your Lord, or is your hope of Paradise focused on other pleasures? How can you cultivate that yearning today?
?
Are you careful to study the exact wording of Allah's Book before forming beliefs, or have you ever clung to an interpretation that suits your desires despite clear linguistic evidence? Will you commit to seeking knowledge with precision?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment. Your eyes will either see Him or not. Does that reality change how you prioritize your life right now? What one distraction can you remove this week to focus on that ultimate meeting?

Poetic Evidence: Vision Even in Metaphorical Contexts
The author cites two poetic lines and discusses interpretations of 'death' to support the vision interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two lines of Arabic poetry reinforce that 'naẓar' with 'ilā' indicates vision, even when the object is death or a powerful figure.

Core Idea
Poetry confirms: 'naẓar' with 'ilā' means seeing, not waiting, even metaphorically.

The author continues to strengthen his argument by examining two well-known poetic lines. One by Jamil ibn Ma'mar uses 'nāẓir' with 'ilā' to describe looking at a powerful person with humility. Another describes faces looking at death. Both use 'ilā', confirming that the intended meaning is vision, not waiting. The author also addresses interpretations of 'death' in such contexts, showing that even metaphorical usage relies on the concept of visibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its translation. Pay attention to the two poetic examples and the author's comments.
The passage includes Jamil's line and a line about death.
2
Analyze Jamil ibn Ma'mar's verse
Find the line and notice how 'nāẓir' is used with 'ilā'. The author explains that it means vision, as it is directed with 'ilā' and the context implies humility.
The poet looks at the hoped-for person with lowliness, which is a vision, not waiting.
3
Analyze the death-oriented verse
Examine the line about faces looking to death from swords. Note that 'ilā' is used again, so it means looking, not waiting.
The author also discusses interpretations of 'death' to show it is visible.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these examples strengthen the rule that 'naẓar' with 'ilā' is always about seeing, and how the author addresses potential objections by interpreting 'death' as something visible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: إن مراد الشاعر من هذا اليمامة سمي بيوم بكر لأن القتال كان يوم فيه مع بني حنيفة بن يخم رهط مسيلمة الكذاب، وبنو حنيفة بطن من بكر بن وائل فسمي ذلك اليوم يوم بكر لهذا، وعنى بالرحمن مسيلمة الكذاب فإنهم كانوا يسمونه رحمن اليمامة. وبهذا أبطلنا تأويل من قال: معناه ثواب ربها منتظرة لما مر أن النظر المضاف إلى الوجه المعدى إلى المنظور إليه بحرف إلى لن يكون المراد منه نظر الانتظار. فأما إذا أريد به الانتظار فإنه لا يعلق بالوجه ولا يعدى بإلى قال الله تعالى خبرا عن تلك المرأة: فَنَاظِرَةٌ بِمَ يَرْجِعُ الْمُرْسَلُونَ ولم تقيد بالوجه ولا عدي بكلمة إلى لما أريد به الانتظار. وقال الشاعر: فإن بك صدر هذا اليوم ولـى فإن غـ لنـاظره قريـ أي لمنتظرة، فأما قول جميل بن معمر: إني إليك لما وعدت لناظر نظر الذليل إلى العزيز القاهر فإن المراد منه النظر الذي هو رؤية العين لأنه عداه بكلمة إلى فمعناه: إني انظر إليك بالذل والانكسار لأن النظر إلى المأمول على هذا الوجه يبعثه على المسارعة إلى قضاء الحاجة وانجاز الوعد. وفي قول القائل: ويوم بذي قار رأيت وجوههم إلى الموت من وقع السيوف نواظر أراد أيضا نظر الرؤية لما عداه بكلمة إلى. ثم إن بعض الناس قالوا: المراد به حقيقة الموت، وهو عندنا مرئي إذ كل موجود جائز الرؤية على ما نبين – إن شاء الله تعالى - وبعضهم قالوا أراد بالموت أسباب الموت كالضرب والطعن وهي مرئية في العادة والشيء قد يسمى باسم ما يؤول إليه. وبعضهم قال: أراد بالموت الإبطال الذي يوجد الموت بسبب ضربهم وطعنهم وإقدامهم في الهيجاء كمال قال جرير: أنا الموت الذي خُبِّرت عنّ فليس لهارب مني نجا وقال آخر: يأيها الراكب المسجي مطيته سائل بني أسد مــا هــذا الـصـوت وقل لهم بادروا بالعذر والتمسوا قولا يقربكم إنـي أنــا المـوت. والذي يدل على أن حمل الآية على الانتظار فاسد وجهان من المعقول: أحدهما راجع إلى نفس المنتظر فهو أن الثواب يومئذ يكون موجودا لأن الجنة دار وقوع الثواب لا دار الانتظار لذلك موجود في الدنيا، والمعدوم هو الذي ينتظر لا الموجود، وما يقال في مبتذل الكلام انتظر زيدا معناه انتظر حضوره
He said: Indeed, the poet's intent from this al-Yamama was called 'Day of Bakr' because the fighting occurred on that day with Banu Hanifa, the clan of Musaylima the liar, and Banu Hanifa are a branch of Bakr ibn Wa'il, so that day was called 'Day of Bakr' for this reason. And by 'the Most Merciful' he meant Musaylima the liar, for they used to call him 'Rahman of al-Yamama'. And by this we have invalidated the interpretation of those who said: its meaning is 'awaiting the reward of her Lord', since it has passed that 'al-naẓar' attached to the face and directed to the object of sight with the letter 'ilā' cannot have the meaning of 'naẓar al-intiẓār' (the look of waiting). As for when waiting is intended, it is not attached to the face nor directed with 'ilā'. Allah the Exalted said, reporting about that woman: 'So she is watching (fānāẓiratun) with what the messengers return', and it was not restricted to the face nor directed with the word 'ilā' because waiting was intended. And the poet said: 'For if your chest of this day has turned away, then she is waiting (nāẓiruhu) for Qurya', meaning 'waiting'. As for the saying of Jamil ibn Ma'mar: 'I am, since you promised, looking (nāẓir) to you, the look of the lowly to the mighty omnipotent', the intent from it is the look that is vision of the eye because he directed it with the word 'ilā'. So its meaning: I look at you with lowliness and contrition, because looking at the hoped-for in this manner urges him to hasten to fulfill the need and fulfill the promise. And in the saying of the speaker: 'And on the day of Dhi Qār I saw their faces to death from the falling of swords looking'—he also intended the look of vision because he directed it with the word 'ilā'. Then some people said: the intended by it is the reality of death, and it is in our view visible, since every existent is permissible of vision as we will explain—if Allah wills. And some said: they intended by death the causes of death, like striking and stabbing, and they are visible in custom, and a thing may be named after that to which it leads. And some said: they intended by death the annihilation that occurs due to their striking, stabbing, and advancing in the fray, as Jarir said: 'I am death that you were informed of, so no one fleeing from me escapes.' And another said: 'O rider whose mount is journeying, ask the Banu Asad what this sound is, and tell them to hasten with excuse and seek a word that brings you near; indeed, I am death.' And what indicates that applying the verse to waiting is corrupt are two aspects from reason: one of them pertains to the one waiting himself—that reward on that day would exist, because Paradise is the abode of occurrence of reward, not the abode of waiting; therefore it exists in this world, and what is non-existent is what is waited for, not what exists. And what is said in common speech, 'I waited for Zayd', meaning 'I waited for his arrival'.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the interpretation that the word 'nāẓirāt' (in the verse 'toward their Lord looking') means waiting for reward rather than seeing. The author begins by explaining a line of poetry that mentions 'the day of Bakr' and 'the Most Merciful' referring to Musaylima the liar, to show that the word 'naẓar' can be used in contexts of vision. He then directly invalidates the claim that the verse means 'awaiting the reward of her Lord' by stating a linguistic rule: when 'naẓar' is attached to the face (al-wajh) and directed with the preposition 'ilā', it cannot mean waiting. To prove this, he cites a Quranic verse where 'fānāẓiratun' is used without 'ilā' to indicate waiting, and a line of poetry where 'nāẓirah' means waiting without 'ilā'. In contrast, the verse in question uses 'ilā rabbihā nāẓirah' (to their Lord looking), so it must mean seeing. He then examines a line by Jamil ibn Ma'mar that uses 'nāẓir' with 'ilā', arguing that it clearly means vision because it is directed with 'ilā', and the context of supplication reinforces this. Similarly, a line about faces looking to death with swords proves the same point. The author then discusses three interpretations of 'death' in such poetry: as the reality of death (which is visible because all existents are visible), as the causes of death (like striking, which are customarily visible), or as annihilation personified (as in Jarir's poetry). This shows that even metaphorical uses rely on the concept of visibility. Finally, the author introduces two rational proofs that the waiting interpretation is invalid. The first proof is that reward on the Day of Judgment already exists in Paradise; waiting is for something non-existent, but reward exists, so waiting makes no sense. The common phrase 'I waited for Zayd' means waiting for his arrival, not for Zayd himself who already exists. The second proof is cut off at the page break but is introduced as the second of two aspects.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Poetic uses of 'naẓar' with 'ilā' can still mean waiting.
Correction: The author shows that in these examples, 'ilā' is used, so the intended meaning is vision, as confirmed by context and the authors' own explanations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Jamil ibn Ma'mar's use of 'nāẓir' with 'ilā' indicate vision rather than waiting?
A) Because the poet is looking at a hoped-for person, which requires waiting.
B) Because it is directed with 'ilā', and the context implies a humble look, not a wait.
C) Because the word 'nāẓir' in Arabic always means waiting.
D) Because the author says it's a metaphor for waiting.

Key Takeaway
Poetic usage confirms that 'naẓar' with 'ilā' always indicates vision, even when the object is abstract like death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment, does your heart long for that moment with the same humble gaze described in the first poetic line—'the look of the lowly to the mighty omnipotent'? Or have you settled for a distant hope that does not move your soul? Pause and ask yourself: what is the quality of your anticipation—is it mere waiting or an active, humble longing that manifests in your deeds today? Commit to one act today that reflects that longing.
?
The author argues that even abstract death is visible in some sense. If you truly believed that you will see Allah with your own eyes—not as a metaphor but as a real physical vision—how would your daily priorities change? What would you do today that you would not otherwise do? Be specific and concrete in your resolution.
?
The poet Jamil ibn Ma'mar speaks of looking 'with humility and brokenness' to the mighty One. In what area of your life do you still feel self-sufficient or proud before Allah? Write down that area and then perform an act of physical humility in your next prayer (e.g., a longer sujood) as a tangible reminder of your lowliness before Him.

intermediate
4 min
Rational Proof: Reward Exists, So Waiting Is Illogical
The author presents the first rational proof against the waiting interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that the interpretation of 'nāẓirāt' as waiting is invalid because the reward in Paradise already exists, and waiting is only for what is non-existent.

Core Idea
You can't wait for what already exists. Reward exists, so waiting is nonsense.

After providing linguistic and poetic evidence, the author now presents a rational argument. He points out that if 'nāẓirāt' meant awaiting reward, that would imply the reward is not yet present. But Paradise is the abode where reward occurs, not where it is awaited. Reward already exists in the next world; waiting is for something that does not yet exist. The common phrase 'I waited for Zayd' means waiting for his arrival, not for Zayd himself, who already exists. This logical point refutes the waiting interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the rational argument the author presents.
The passage contains the first of two rational proofs.
2
Identify the main premise
Find where the author states that reward on the Day of Judgment exists because Paradise is the place of occurrence, not expectation.
The argument is: waiting is for something that does not exist, but reward exists, so waiting is invalid.
3
Understand the analogy with 'Zayd'
The author uses a common expression: 'I waited for Zayd' means waiting for his arrival, not for Zayd himself. Similarly, if the verse meant waiting, it would be waiting for the reward's arrival, but reward is already present.
This shows that even in everyday language, waiting is for an event, not an existing entity.
4
Reflect on the impact
Consider how this rational proof complements the linguistic evidence and strengthens the case that 'nāẓirāt' must mean seeing.

According to the rational proof, why is the waiting interpretation invalid?
A) Because waiting requires time, and time does not exist in the afterlife.

Psychological Incompatibility of Waiting with Paradise Bliss
Imam Nasafi refutes the 'waiting' interpretation by invoking the blissful state of Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi argues that interpreting 'looking' as 'waiting' contradicts the state of the people of Paradise.

Core Idea
Waiting implies anxiety, which cannot coexist with perfect bliss.

Imam Nasafi begins his refutation by focusing on the psychological state of the people of Paradise. He argues that the concept of waiting inherently involves anxiety, discomfort, and a sense of incompleteness, which are diametrically opposed to the perfect bliss and peace that the Quran attributes to the inhabitants of Paradise. The verse itself describes their faces as fresh and radiant, a description of those who have already attained their reward, not those still in a state of anticipation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall argument without overanalyzing.
Pay attention to the proverb 'Waiting is a red death'.
2
Identify the two main points
Note that the passage argues first that waiting would cause discomfort in Paradise, and second that the Quranic description of faces as 'fresh' indicates they have already attained reward, not that they are waiting.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author uses a strong proverb. What does it tell us about the nature of waiting? How does this conflict with the idea of eternal bliss?
The point is that waiting spoils enjoyment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقُدُومِهِ، أَلَا تَرَى لَوْ فَسَّرَ فَقَالَ: انْتَظِرْ حُضُورَهُ كَانَ مُسْتَقِيمًا وَلَوْ قَالَ: انْتَظِرْ وُجُودَهُ كَانَ فَاسِدًا، فَدَلَّ أَنَّ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ بَاطِلٌ. وَأَمَّا الرَّاجِعُ إِلَى حَالِ الْمُنْتَظِرِ فَهُوَ أَنَّ وَلِيَّ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فِي الْجَنَّةِ مَصُونٌ عَمَّا يُوجِبُ اعْتِرَاءَ وَحْشَةٍ فِي صَدْرِهِ أَوْ ضِيقٍ وَقَلَقٍ فِي قَلْبِهِ، وَفِي الِانْتِظَارِ ذَلِكَ وَتَنْغِيصٌ لِلنِّعْمَةِ، وَقَدْ قِيلَ فِي الْمَثَلِ السَّائِرِ: الِانْتِظَارُ مَوْتٌ أَحْمَرُ. وَهَذَا مِمَّا لَا يَلِيقُ بِحَالِ أَهْلِ الْجَنَّةِ، أَلَا تَرَى كَيْفَ وَصَفَهُمْ بِنَضَارَةِ الْوُجُوهِ بِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ وَصْفُ مَنْ نَالَ الثَّوَابَ لَا وَصْفُ مَنْ هُوَ بَعْدُ يُقَاسِي أَلَمَ الِانْتِظَارِ وَكَرْبَهُ. يُحَقِّقُهُ أَنَّ النَّظَرَ مُضَافٌ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لَا إِلَى غَيْرِهِ فَلَا يَجُوزُ صَرْفُهُ إِلَى غَيْرِهِ، وَلَوْ جَازَ ذَا لَجَازَ صَرْفُ قَوْلِهِ: اعْبُدُوا رَبَّكُمْ إِلَى غَيْرِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى. وَحَقِيقَةُ هَذَا أَنَّ إِضْمَارَ الْمُضَافِ وَإِقَامَةَ الْمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ مَقَامَ الْمُضَافِ إِنَّمَا يَجُوزُ عِنْدَ تَعَيُّنِ الْمُضَافِ فِي نَفْسِهِ بِدَلِيلٍ مِنَ الدَّلَائِلِ فَيَحْصُلُ فَائِدَةُ الْكَلَامِ الْإِفْهَامُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: وَاسْأَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ الَّتِي كُنَّا فِيهَا، أَيْ أَهْلَ الْقَرْيَةِ، لِأَنَّ السُّؤَالَ لِلْجَوَابِ وَلَا جَوَابَ يُنْتَظَرُ مِنَ الْأَبْنِيَةِ وَالْحِيطَانِ وَشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْجَمَادَاتِ، فَعُلِمَ أَنَّ الْمُرَادَ مِنْهُ مَنْ يُنْتَظَرُ مِنْهُ الْجَوَابُ، وَقَدِ اخْتَصَّ بِذَلِكَ الْأَهْلُ. فَأَمَّا عِنْدَ انْعِدَامِ دَلِيلِ تَعَيُّنِ بَعْضِ مَا يُضَافُ فَلَا يَجُوزُ الْإِضْمَارُ لِانْعِدَامِ الْفَائِدَةِ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ قَائِلًا لَوْ قَالَ: رَأَيْتُ زَيْدًا وَأَرَادَ بِهِ عِمَامَتَهُ أَوْ قَمِيصَهُ أَوْ دَابَّتَهُ أَوْ غُلَامَهُ أَوْ دَارَهُ، كَانَ مُخْطِئًا فِي ذَلِكَ لِاسْتِوَاءِ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءِ وَمَا سِوَاهَا أَيْضًا فِي صِحَّةِ الْإِضَافَةِ إِلَى زَيْدٍ وَانْعِدَامِ دَلِيلِ التَّعْيِينِ، فَكَانَ الْمُتَكَلِّمُ بِهِ الْمُرِيدُ بَعْضَ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءِ مِنْ غَيْرِ دَلِيلِ التَّعْيِينِ مُتَكَلِّمًا بِمَا لَا فَائِدَةَ فِيهِ فَيُعَدُّ سَفِيهًا جَاهِلًا. ثُمَّ مَا يَصِحُّ إِضَافَتُهُ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى أَشْيَاءُ كَثِيرَةٌ مِنْ نَحْوِ الْجَنَّةِ وَالْعَرْشِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ وَالْأَنْبِيَاءِ وَالرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِمُ السَّلَامُ وَأَصْحَابِ الرُّسُلِ وَالْأَوْلِيَاءِ وَعِبَادِ اللَّهِ الصَّالِحِينَ وَالدُّورِ وَالْقُصُورِ وَالْحُورِ الْعِينِ وَالْغِلْمَانِ وَالْبُسُطِ وَالْجِبَالِ وَالْأَوَانِي وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِمَّا لَا يُحِيطُ بِهِ الْعَدُّ وَالْإِحْصَاءُ، وَإِنْ بُولِغَ فِي الِاسْتِقْصَاءِ، فَتَعْيِينُ الْبَعْضِ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ يُوجِبُ التَّعْيِينَ غَيْرُ سَائِغٍ فِي اللُّغَةِ وَخُرُوجٌ فِي الْخِطَابِ عَنْ عَادَاتِ الْحُكَمَاءِ، فَحَمْلُ كَلَامِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى عَلَى هَذَا فَاسِدٌ لَا يَجُوزُ الْمَصِيرُ إِلَيْهِ، وَفِي التَّعَلُّقِ بِالْآيَةِ وَالِاعْتِرَاضِ عَلَيْهِ كَلَامٌ كَثِيرٌ وَفِيمَا ذَكَرْتُ كِفَايَةٌ.
And His coming. Do you not see that if he interpreted and said: 'Wait for his presence' it would be correct, and if he said: 'Wait for his existence' it would be corrupt? So this indicates that this interpretation is invalid. As for what refers to the state of the one waiting, it is that the friend of Allah the Exalted in Paradise is protected from what would cause the occurrence of loneliness in his chest or constriction and anxiety in his heart, and in waiting there is that and the spoiling of bliss. It has been said in the current proverb: 'Waiting is a red death.' This is not befitting the state of the people of Paradise. Do you not see how He described them with freshness of faces by His statement: 'Faces on that day are fresh, looking toward their Lord.' That is a description of one who has attained reward, not a description of one who still feels the pain and distress of waiting. Its verification is that 'looking' is added to Allah the Exalted, not to other than Him, so it is not permissible to divert it to other than Him. If that were permissible, then diverting His statement: 'Worship your Lord' to other than Allah would be permissible. The reality of this is that the elision of the added term and the substitution of the added-to term in its place is only permissible when the added term is specified in itself by a proof among proofs, such that the benefit of the speech, which is understanding, is achieved, as in His statement: 'And ask the town which we were in,' meaning the people of the town, because asking is for an answer, and no answer is expected from buildings, walls, or any inanimate objects. So it is known that the intended is one from whom an answer is expected, and that is specific to the inhabitants. As for when the proof for specifying some of what could be added is absent, then elision is not permissible due to the absence of benefit. Do you not see that if a speaker said: 'I saw Zayd' and intended by it his turban, or his shirt, or his mount, or his slave, or his house, he would be mistaken in that because of the equal possibility of these things and others in the validity of attribution to Zayd and the absence of a specifying proof? So the speaker who intends some of these things without a specifying proof speaks with no benefit and is considered foolish and ignorant. Then, there are many things that can be added to Allah the Exalted, such as Paradise, the Throne, the angels, the prophets, the messengers, peace be upon them, the companions of the messengers, the saints, the righteous servants of Allah, houses, palaces, houris, boys, carpets, mountains, vessels, and other things that cannot be encompassed by counting or enumeration, even if one goes to extremes in seeking. So specifying some without a proof necessitating specification is not permissible in language and is a departure in speech from the customs of the wise. Therefore, interpreting the speech of Allah the Exalted in this way is corrupt and not permissible to adopt. And there is much discussion regarding the attachment to the verse and the objection to it, but what I have mentioned is sufficient.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the interpretation that the word 'nazar' (looking) in the Quranic verse 'Faces on that day are fresh, looking toward their Lord' means 'waiting' rather than literal vision. The author (Imam Nasafi) presents two main arguments: First, from the perspective of the state of the people of Paradise. He argues that waiting implies anxiety and distress, which are incompatible with the bliss of Paradise. The proverb 'Waiting is a red death' emphasizes the pain of waiting. The verse describes faces as 'fresh' (nadira), which is a description of those who have already attained reward, not of those still awaiting. Second, he provides a linguistic argument: The word 'nazar' is grammatically added (mudaf) to Allah, so it must refer to looking at Allah Himself. If it were to be interpreted as waiting for something else, the same principle would allow diverting other clear texts like 'Worship your Lord' to mean worshipping something else, which is absurd. He then elaborates on the grammatical rule of 'idmar al-mudaf' (elision of the added term). Such elision is only valid when the omitted term is clearly specified by evidence, as in 'Ask the town' meaning 'the people of the town,' because asking expects an answer from people, not buildings. Without specifying evidence, elision leads to ambiguity and aimless speech, as in 'I saw Zayd' intending his turban or shirt without clarification. Applying this to the verse, if 'nazar' were to be interpreted as 'waiting', one would have to arbitrarily specify what is being awaited among many possibilities (e.g., Paradise, Throne, angels, etc.). Since no clear evidence specifies one among these, such an interpretation is linguistically invalid and against the customary usage of wise people. Therefore, the interpretation that 'looking' means 'waiting' is corrupt and must be rejected.

According to Imam Nasafi, why is waiting incompatible with the state of the people of Paradise?
A) Because waiting implies anxiety and spoils bliss.
B) Because they are already in Paradise and have no need to wait.
C) Because the verse uses the word 'looking' not 'waiting'.
D) Because waiting is a form of punishment.

Key Takeaway
The people of Paradise are described as already enjoying the vision of Allah, free from any trace of waiting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself 'waiting' for Allah's reward or vision as if it is distant? How does this lesson challenge that inner impatience, and what can you do today to live as if His presence is already near?
?
The people of Paradise are described as having freshness of faces and looking to their Lord, free from anxiety. Is your current state filled with the 'red death' of waiting for something, or are you experiencing the peace of already being in His presence? What step will you take to shift from waiting to beholding?
?
If 'waiting is a red death,' what worldly anticipation or future hope is actually robbing you of present intimacy with Allah? Will you release it now and turn your gaze towards Him?

Unseen Matters
intermediate
8 min
Linguistic Invalidity of Interpreting 'Nazar' as 'Waiting'
Imam Nasafi employs the linguistic rule of idmar al-mudaf to refute the interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic rule of idmar al-mudaf (elision of the added term) and why it invalidates interpreting 'nazar' as 'waiting'.

Core Idea
Elision in Arabic must be guided by clear evidence; otherwise, speech becomes meaningless.

Imam Nasafi then provides a sophisticated linguistic argument. He establishes that the word 'nazar' is grammatically added (mudaf) to Allah, so it directly refers to looking at Allah. To interpret it as 'waiting' would require an elision of an unstated object. He explains the rule that such elision is only valid when the omitted term is clearly determined by evidence, giving Quranic examples and a counterexample. Applying this to the verse, since there are many things that could be added to Allah without specification, the interpretation is linguistically unsound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the grammatical terminology: مضاف, إضمار, etc.
This passage is more technical than the previous one.
2
Understand the rule of idmar al-mudaf
The author explains that elision of the added term is only permissible when the omitted term is specified by evidence. He gives the Quranic example 'Ask the town' meaning 'the people of the town'.
The evidence here is that only people can answer questions.
3
Analyze the counterexample
The example 'I saw Zayd' intending his turban or shirt is invalid because there is no evidence specifying which item. This shows the necessity of a specifying proof.
4
Apply the rule to the verse
The author lists many things that can be added to Allah. Since there is no evidence to specify which one is meant by 'waiting', the interpretation fails.
Without evidence, the elision leads to ambiguity.
5
Conclude
The interpretation is linguistically invalid and goes against the customs of wise speakers.

وَقُدُومِهِ، أَلَا تَرَى لَوْ فَسَّرَ فَقَالَ: انْتَظِرْ حُضُورَهُ كَانَ مُسْتَقِيمًا وَلَوْ قَالَ: انْتَظِرْ وُجُودَهُ كَانَ فَاسِدًا، فَدَلَّ أَنَّ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ بَاطِلٌ. وَأَمَّا الرَّاجِعُ إِلَى حَالِ الْمُنْتَظِرِ فَهُوَ أَنَّ وَلِيَّ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فِي الْجَنَّةِ مَصُونٌ عَمَّا يُوجِبُ اعْتِرَاءَ وَحْشَةٍ فِي صَدْرِهِ أَوْ ضِيقٍ وَقَلَقٍ فِي قَلْبِهِ، وَفِي الِانْتِظَارِ ذَلِكَ وَتَنْغِيصٌ لِلنِّعْمَةِ، وَقَدْ قِيلَ فِي الْمَثَلِ السَّائِرِ: الِانْتِظَارُ مَوْتٌ أَحْمَرُ. وَهَذَا مِمَّا لَا يَلِيقُ بِحَالِ أَهْلِ الْجَنَّةِ، أَلَا تَرَى كَيْفَ وَصَفَهُمْ بِنَضَارَةِ الْوُجُوهِ بِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ وَصْفُ مَنْ نَالَ الثَّوَابَ لَا وَصْفُ مَنْ هُوَ بَعْدُ يُقَاسِي أَلَمَ الِانْتِظَارِ وَكَرْبَهُ. يُحَقِّقُهُ أَنَّ النَّظَرَ مُضَافٌ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لَا إِلَى غَيْرِهِ فَلَا يَجُوزُ صَرْفُهُ إِلَى غَيْرِهِ، وَلَوْ جَازَ ذَا لَجَازَ صَرْفُ قَوْلِهِ: اعْبُدُوا رَبَّكُمْ إِلَى غَيْرِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى. وَحَقِيقَةُ هَذَا أَنَّ إِضْمَارَ الْمُضَافِ وَإِقَامَةَ الْمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ مَقَامَ الْمُضَافِ إِنَّمَا يَجُوزُ عِنْدَ تَعَيُّنِ الْمُضَافِ فِي نَفْسِهِ بِدَلِيلٍ مِنَ الدَّلَائِلِ فَيَحْصُلُ فَائِدَةُ الْكَلَامِ الْإِفْهَامُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: وَاسْأَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ الَّتِي كُنَّا فِيهَا، أَيْ أَهْلَ الْقَرْيَةِ، لِأَنَّ السُّؤَالَ لِلْجَوَابِ وَلَا جَوَابَ يُنْتَظَرُ مِنَ الْأَبْنِيَةِ وَالْحِيطَانِ وَشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْجَمَادَاتِ، فَعُلِمَ أَنَّ الْمُرَادَ مِنْهُ مَنْ يُنْتَظَرُ مِنْهُ الْجَوَابُ، وَقَدِ اخْتَصَّ بِذَلِكَ الْأَهْلُ. فَأَمَّا عِنْدَ انْعِدَامِ دَلِيلِ تَعَيُّنِ بَعْضِ مَا يُضَافُ فَلَا يَجُوزُ الْإِضْمَارُ لِانْعِدَامِ الْفَائِدَةِ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ قَائِلًا لَوْ قَالَ: رَأَيْتُ زَيْدًا وَأَرَادَ بِهِ عِمَامَتَهُ أَوْ قَمِيصَهُ أَوْ دَابَّتَهُ أَوْ غُلَامَهُ أَوْ دَارَهُ، كَانَ مُخْطِئًا فِي ذَلِكَ لِاسْتِوَاءِ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءِ وَمَا سِوَاهَا أَيْضًا فِي صِحَّةِ الْإِضَافَةِ إِلَى زَيْدٍ وَانْعِدَامِ دَلِيلِ التَّعْيِينِ، فَكَانَ الْمُتَكَلِّمُ بِهِ الْمُرِيدُ بَعْضَ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءِ مِنْ غَيْرِ دَلِيلِ التَّعْيِينِ مُتَكَلِّمًا بِمَا لَا فَائِدَةَ فِيهِ فَيُعَدُّ سَفِيهًا جَاهِلًا. ثُمَّ مَا يَصِحُّ إِضَافَتُهُ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى أَشْيَاءُ كَثِيرَةٌ مِنْ نَحْوِ الْجَنَّةِ وَالْعَرْشِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ وَالْأَنْبِيَاءِ وَالرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِمُ السَّلَامُ وَأَصْحَابِ الرُّسُلِ وَالْأَوْلِيَاءِ وَعِبَادِ اللَّهِ الصَّالِحِينَ وَالدُّورِ وَالْقُصُورِ وَالْحُورِ الْعِينِ وَالْغِلْمَانِ وَالْبُسُطِ وَالْجِبَالِ وَالْأَوَانِي وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِمَّا لَا يُحِيطُ بِهِ الْعَدُّ وَالْإِحْصَاءُ، وَإِنْ بُولِغَ فِي الِاسْتِقْصَاءِ، فَتَعْيِينُ الْبَعْضِ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ يُوجِبُ التَّعْيِينَ غَيْرُ سَائِغٍ فِي اللُّغَةِ وَخُرُوجٌ فِي الْخِطَابِ عَنْ عَادَاتِ الْحُكَمَاءِ، فَحَمْلُ كَلَامِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى عَلَى هَذَا فَاسِدٌ لَا يَجُوزُ الْمَصِيرُ إِلَيْهِ، وَفِي التَّعَلُّقِ بِالْآيَةِ وَالِاعْتِرَاضِ عَلَيْهِ كَلَامٌ كَثِيرٌ وَفِيمَا ذَكَرْتُ كِفَايَةٌ.
And His coming. Do you not see that if he interpreted and said: 'Wait for his presence' it would be correct, and if he said: 'Wait for his existence' it would be corrupt? So this indicates that this interpretation is invalid. As for what refers to the state of the one waiting, it is that the friend of Allah the Exalted in Paradise is protected from what would cause the occurrence of loneliness in his chest or constriction and anxiety in his heart, and in waiting there is that and the spoiling of bliss. It has been said in the current proverb: 'Waiting is a red death.' This is not befitting the state of the people of Paradise. Do you not see how He described them with freshness of faces by His statement: 'Faces on that day are fresh, looking toward their Lord.' That is a description of one who has attained reward, not a description of one who still feels the pain and distress of waiting. Its verification is that 'looking' is added to Allah the Exalted, not to other than Him, so it is not permissible to divert it to other than Him. If that were permissible, then diverting His statement: 'Worship your Lord' to other than Allah would be permissible. The reality of this is that the elision of the added term and the substitution of the added-to term in its place is only permissible when the added term is specified in itself by a proof among proofs, such that the benefit of the speech, which is understanding, is achieved, as in His statement: 'And ask the town which we were in,' meaning the people of the town, because asking is for an answer, and no answer is expected from buildings, walls, or any inanimate objects. So it is known that the intended is one from whom an answer is expected, and that is specific to the inhabitants. As for when the proof for specifying some of what could be added is absent, then elision is not permissible due to the absence of benefit. Do you not see that if a speaker said: 'I saw Zayd' and intended by it his turban, or his shirt, or his mount, or his slave, or his house, he would be mistaken in that because of the equal possibility of these things and others in the validity of attribution to Zayd and the absence of a specifying proof? So the speaker who intends some of these things without a specifying proof speaks with no benefit and is considered foolish and ignorant. Then, there are many things that can be added to Allah the Exalted, such as Paradise, the Throne, the angels, the prophets, the messengers, peace be upon them, the companions of the messengers, the saints, the righteous servants of Allah, houses, palaces, houris, boys, carpets, mountains, vessels, and other things that cannot be encompassed by counting or enumeration, even if one goes to extremes in seeking. So specifying some without a proof necessitating specification is not permissible in language and is a departure in speech from the customs of the wise. Therefore, interpreting the speech of Allah the Exalted in this way is corrupt and not permissible to adopt. And there is much discussion regarding the attachment to the verse and the objection to it, but what I have mentioned is sufficient.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the interpretation that the word 'nazar' (looking) in the Quranic verse 'Faces on that day are fresh, looking toward their Lord' means 'waiting' rather than literal vision. The author (Imam Nasafi) presents two main arguments: First, from the perspective of the state of the people of Paradise. He argues that waiting implies anxiety and distress, which are incompatible with the bliss of Paradise. The proverb 'Waiting is a red death' emphasizes the pain of waiting. The verse describes faces as 'fresh' (nadira), which is a description of those who have already attained reward, not of those still awaiting. Second, he provides a linguistic argument: The word 'nazar' is grammatically added (mudaf) to Allah, so it must refer to looking at Allah Himself. If it were to be interpreted as waiting for something else, the same principle would allow diverting other clear texts like 'Worship your Lord' to mean worshipping something else, which is absurd. He then elaborates on the grammatical rule of 'idmar al-mudaf' (elision of the added term). Such elision is only valid when the omitted term is clearly specified by evidence, as in 'Ask the town' meaning 'the people of the town,' because asking expects an answer from people, not buildings. Without specifying evidence, elision leads to ambiguity and aimless speech, as in 'I saw Zayd' intending his turban or shirt without clarification. Applying this to the verse, if 'nazar' were to be interpreted as 'waiting', one would have to arbitrarily specify what is being awaited among many possibilities (e.g., Paradise, Throne, angels, etc.). Since no clear evidence specifies one among these, such an interpretation is linguistically invalid and against the customary usage of wise people. Therefore, the interpretation that 'looking' means 'waiting' is corrupt and must be rejected.

Key Terms
النظر
المضاف
الإضمار
المضاف إليه
التعيين
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to interpret 'nazar' as 'waiting' because the object is understood from context.
Correction: The object is not understood; there is no clear evidence to determine what is being waited for among countless possibilities.

Checkpoint
What is the main linguistic rule that Imam Nasafi uses to refute the interpretation of 'looking' as 'waiting'?
A) The word 'nazar' is always literal.
B) Elision of the added term requires specifying evidence.
C) The Quranic example shows that elision is always allowed.
D) The interpretation leads to polytheism.

Key Takeaway
Without a specifying proof, diverting the word 'nazar' from its direct meaning is linguistically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself tempted to reinterpret a clear verse of Allah to fit your own comfort or desires, what specifying proof do you have? Are you willing to admit that without evidence, your interpretation may be mere speech with no benefit, and you may be like the one who foolishly says 'I saw Zayd' but means his turban? Commit today to always ask: 'Where is my evidence from the Quran and Sunnah?'
?
This lesson shows that careless speech about Allah is not trivial—it can render the speaker foolish and ignorant. Think about times you have spoken about Allah's attributes or actions without solid knowledge. How does this realization weigh on your heart? What step will you take to ensure your words about Allah are always based on clear, unambiguous proof?
?
The example of 'Ask the town' teaches that valid interpretation requires a rational link. Examine your own beliefs: are there any doctrines you hold that lack a rational connection to the texts? If so, will you have the courage to abandon them and seek only what is linguistically sound, even if it is less comfortable?

The Verse of Increase: Proof for the Vision of Allah
Imam Nasafi uses Qur'an 10:26 and its interpretation as proof for the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses the verse 'For those who do good is the best and an increase' as a proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, and why the interpretation of 'increase' as vision is considered sound.

Core Idea
The 'increase' in the Qur'an points to the vision of Allah, affirmed by its widespread acceptance.

This lesson introduces the third proof for ru'ya from Tabirat al-Adilla. Imam Nasafi cites a Qur'anic verse and argues that the interpretation of 'increase' as the vision of Allah is not arbitrary but relies on its prevalence among the early community. We will examine the verse, the reported interpretation, and the reasoning that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse and its interpretation
Read the verse and the reported interpretation that 'ziyada' refers to the vision of Allah.
The verse is from Surah Yunus: 26.
2
Understand the argument from apparent meaning
Notice that the author argues that if the belief in vision were not well-known, the verse could not have been interpreted that way.
This is a counterfactual reasoning.
3
Connect to consensus
See how this argument leads to the claim of consensus among the Companions.
The prevalence of the interpretation implies consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دَلِيلٌ ثَالِثٌ عَلَى جَوَازِ الرُّؤْيَةِ. وَلَنَا أَيْضًا قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ وَجَاءَ خَبَرٌ إِنَّ الزِّيَادَةَ هِيَ النَّظَرُ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَقَدْ تُحْمَلُ غَيْرُ ذَلِكَ مِمَّا جَاءَ فِيهِ التَّفْسِيرُ، لَكِنَّهُ لَوْلَا أَنَّ الْقَوْلَ بِالرُّؤْيَةِ كَانَ أَمْرًا ظَاهِرًا وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ لَمْ يَحْتَمِلْ صَرْفَ ظَاهِرٍ لَمْ يَجِئْ فِيهَا إِلَيْهَا. وَهَذَا فِي الْحَاصِلِ اسْتِدْلَالٌ بِإِجْمَاعِ الصَّحَابَةِ وَشُيُوعِ الْقَوْلِ بِالرُّؤْيَةِ فِيهِمْ. وَذَكَرَ الشَّيْخُ أَبُو عَبْدِ اللَّهِ مُحَمَّدُ بْنُ عَلِيٍّ التِّرْمِذِيُّ فِي تَصْنِيفٍ لَهُ سَمَّاهُ مَسْأَلَةَ سُلُوكِ أَهْلِ الْعَدْلِ بَيْنَ الْمُشَبِّهَةِ وَالْمُعَطِّلَةِ فَقَالَ: اتَّفَقَتْ عَلَى حَدِيثِ الرُّؤْيَةِ عِدَّةٌ مِنْ أَصْحَابِ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ كُلُّهُمْ أَئِمَّةٌ، مِنْهُمْ: ابْنُ عُمَرَ، وَابْنُ مَسْعُودٍ، وَابْنُ عَبَّاسٍ، وَصُهَيْبٌ، وَأَنَسُ بْنُ مَالِكٍ، وَأَبُو مُوسَى الْأَشْعَرِيُّ، وَأَبُو هُرَيْرَةَ، وَأَبُو سَعِيدٍ الْخُدْرِيُّ، وَعَمَّارُ بْنُ يَاسِرٍ، وَجَابِرُ بْنُ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ، وَمُعَاذُ بْنُ جَبَلٍ، وَثَوْبَانُ، وَعَمَّارُ بْنُ رُوَيْبَةَ الثَّقَفِيُّ، وَحُذَيْفَةُ، عَنْ أَبِي بَكْرٍ الصِّدِّيقِ، وَزَيْدُ بْنُ ثَابِتٍ، وَجَرِيرُ بْنُ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ، وَأَبُو أُمَامَةَ، وَبُرَيْدَةُ الْأَسْلَمِيُّ، وَأَبُو بَرْزَةَ، وَعَبْدُ اللَّهِ بْنُ الْحَارِثِ بْنِ جَزْءٍ الزُّبَيْدِيُّ، أَحَدٌ وَعِشْرُونَ رَجُلًا مِنْ أَصْحَابِ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ.
A third proof for the permissibility of vision. We also have His statement: 'For those who do good is the best and an increase.' And a report has come that the increase is the vision of Allah the Exalted. It may also carry other meanings that have come in the interpretation, but if it were not that the statement of vision was a manifest matter, then it would not have been possible to divert an apparent meaning that did not come to it. This, in summary, is an argument by the consensus of the Companions and the prevalence of the statement of vision among them. And Sheikh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Ali al-Tirmidhi mentioned in a work of his titled 'Mas'alat Suluk Ahl al-Adl bayn al-Mushabbiha wa al-Mu'attila' and said: 'Several of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, agreed upon the hadith of vision, all of them are imams. Among them: Ibn Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Suhayb, Anas ibn Malik, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Abu Hurayra, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, Ammar ibn Yasir, Jabir ibn Abdullah, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Thawban, Ammar ibn Ruwayba al-Thaqafi, Hudhayfa, from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, Zayd ibn Thabit, Jarir ibn Abdullah, Abu Umama, Burayda al-Aslami, Abu Barza, and Abdullah ibn al-Harith ibn Jaz' al-Zubaydi—twenty-one men from the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace.'
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife. The author cites the Quranic verse 'For those who do good is the best (al-husna) and an increase (ziyada)' (Surah Yunus: 26). He states that a report (khabar) interprets 'al-ziyada' as the vision (nazar) of Allah. While there are other interpretations, he argues that if the belief in vision were not already an apparent (zahir) and well-known matter among the early community, it would not have been possible for this verse to be interpreted as referring to vision. In other words, the fact that this interpretation exists and is widespread indicates its strength and acceptance. He characterizes this as an argument from the consensus (ijma') of the Companions and the prevalence of the belief in vision among them. To support this, he quotes Sheikh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Ali al-Tirmidhi (not the famous hadith scholar but a Sufi figure from Khurasan) from his work 'Mas'alat Suluk Ahl al-Adl bayn al-Mushabbiha wa al-Mu'attila'. Al-Tirmidhi lists twenty-one Companions who agreed upon the hadith of vision, all of whom are considered imams (authorities). The list includes prominent Companions like Ibn Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Abu Hurayra, Anas ibn Malik, and others. This enumeration demonstrates the widespread transmission and acceptance of the hadith of vision, reinforcing the doctrinal position that seeing Allah is possible and promised for the believers in Paradise.

Key Terms
الزيادة
النظر
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from consensus here relies on a formal juridical consensus (ijma') like that of scholars on a legal ruling.
Correction: This consensus is not a formal juridical consensus but the widespread acceptance and transmission of the hadith of ru'ya among the Companions, indicating its authenticity.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Nasafi consider the interpretation of 'increase' as vision to be valid?
A) Because the verse explicitly says so.
B) Because the interpretation was widespread among the Companions, indicating the belief was already manifest.
C) Because there is a consensus of all Muslims on this interpretation.
D) Because no other interpretation exists.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'increase' interpreted as vision of Allah is a valid proof because its acceptance among the Companions shows its authenticity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'For those who do good is the best and an increase,' does your heart long for that 'increase' as the vision of Allah, or do you find yourself satisfied with the lesser reward of Paradise? What does your answer reveal about your current relationship with your Creator?
?
The Companions accepted the interpretation of this verse as referring to seeing Allah with such certainty that it became a consensus. Do you share that same certainty in your heart about the reality of seeing Allah? If not, what is the root of your doubt, and what step will you take today to strengthen that conviction?
?
If you were standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment and He asked you why you did not strive to believe in His promise of vision, what would you say? How would you answer for any hesitation or denial, knowing that the early Muslims embraced this truth with full submission?

The Consensus of the Companions on the Vision of Allah
Imam Nasafi cites al-Tirmidhi's list of 21 Companions who agreed on the hadith of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses the explicit listing of twenty-one Companions by al-Tirmidhi to demonstrate the widespread transmission and acceptance of the hadith of vision, strengthening the proof for ru'ya.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is affirmed by the consensus of twenty-one leading Companions.

In the previous lesson, we saw the argument from the verse and the prevalence of the belief in vision. Here, Imam Nasafi provides explicit evidence of that prevalence by quoting Sheikh al-Tirmidhi, who lists twenty-one Companions who transmitted the hadith of vision. This list serves as a powerful demonstration of consensus among the Companions, all of whom are considered authorities.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of Companions
Read the list and notice the names. All are considered imams (authorities).
The list includes Ibn Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, and others.
2
Understand the significance of the list
The author uses this list to show that the hadith of vision is widely transmitted and accepted by the most reliable Companions.
This reinforces the earlier argument from prevalence.
3
Connect to the overall proof
Realize that this list establishes a strong case for ru'ya through the consensus of the Companions.
Consensus of the Companions is a strong proof in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
These twenty-one Companions, all imams, agreed on the vision of Allah. Their certainty came from direct knowledge of the Prophet. You, however, rely on transmitted reports. Is your faith in this unseen reality as firm as theirs? In what specific area of your life does doubt about the unseen surface, and what will you do today to strengthen that conviction?
?
The Companions did not just agree; they were the most authoritative voices of Islam. If they were so united on this belief, what does your own hesitation or indifference toward the vision of Allah reveal about your heart? Are you truly living as if you will see Him? What practical step can you take this week to make the vision of Allah a more present hope in your daily life?
?
Al-Tirmidhi wrote to steer between two dangerous extremes. Are there areas in your faith where you lean toward excessive materialization (tashbih) or complete denial (ta'til) when thinking about Allah? How does the Companions' balanced affirmation of seeing Allah without resembling creation challenge you to recalibrate your own understanding?

Third Scriptural Proof for the Vision of Allah (Ru'ya)
Nasafi presents third proof for ru'ya from Quran and companion consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the Quranic verse 'ziyada' and the consensus of the Companions to argue for the permissibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Consensus on ru'ya among Companions proves both occurrence and permissibility.

Nasafi now presents a third proof, combining a Quranic verse with the consensus of the Companions. He argues that the widespread acceptance of ru'ya hadiths among the early Muslims establishes not only that vision of Allah occurs in the Hereafter but also that it is permissible. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage and the English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a verse, its interpretation, a list of narrators, and a concluding inference.
2
Identify the two main pillars
Nasafi uses the Quranic verse (ziyada) and the consensus of the Companions. Identify how each supports the permissibility of ru'ya.
The verse provides scriptural basis; consensus provides confirmatory evidence.
3
Understand the logical inference
Nasafi argues that if ru'ya is established as occurring (thubut), then its permissibility (jawaz) is automatically proven. Reflect on why that inference holds.
This is a key kalam principle: actual occurrence implies possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث على جواز الرؤية: ولنا أيضا قوله تعالى: لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ وجاء خبر إن الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله تعالى وقد تحمل غير ذلك مما جاء فيه التفسير لكنه لولا أن القول بالرؤية كان أمرا ظاهرا وإلا لكان لم يحتمل صرف ظاهر لم يجئ فيها إليها، وهذا في الحاصل استدلال بإجماع الصحابة وشيوع القول بالرؤية فيهم. وذكر الشيخ أبو عبد الله محمد بن على الترمذي في تصنيف له سماه مسألة سلوك أهل العدل بين المشبهة والمعطلة فقال: اتفقت على حديث الرؤية عدة من أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كلهم أئمة منهم: ابن عمر وابن مسعود وابن عباس وصهيب وأنس ابن مالك وأبو موسى الأشعري وأبو هريرة وأبو سعيد الخدري وعمار بن ياسر وجابر بن عبد الله ومعاذ بن جبل وثوبان وعمار بن روبية الثقفي وحذيفة عن أبي بكر الصديق وزيد بن ثابت وجرير بن عبد الله وأبو إمامة وبريدة الأسلمي وأبو برزة وعبد الله بن الحارث بن جز الزبيدي أحد وعشرون رجلا من أصحاب رسول الله عليه السلام كلهم رووا عن رسول الله عليه السلام في إثبات الرؤية فمن ردها فقد قصد تكذيب هؤلاء في الأحاديث والدلائل السمعية، وفي هذا الباب كثرة وقد ثبتت بهذه الدلائل رؤية الله تعالى ولا ثبوت لما لا جواز له فثبت في ضمن الثبوت جواز الرؤية.
Third proof for the permissibility of vision: And we also have His saying, the Exalted: 'For those who have done good is the best [reward] and more' (Surah Yunus 10:26). And there came a report that the 'more' is the vision of Allah the Exalted. And it may be interpreted as other than that which has come in the commentary, but were it not that the statement about vision was an apparent matter, otherwise it would not have been possible to divert the apparent meaning from it. And this, in summary, is an inference by the consensus of the Companions and the prevalence of the statement about vision among them. And Shaykh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Ali al-Tirmidhi mentioned in a work of his called 'Mas'ala Suluk Ahl al-Adl bayn al-Mushabbiha wa al-Mu'attila' and said: 'Several of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, all of them leaders, agreed upon the hadith of vision, among them: Ibn Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Suhayb, Anas ibn Malik, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Abu Hurayra, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, Ammar ibn Yasir, Jabir ibn Abdullah, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Thawban, Ammar ibn Rubayya al-Thaqafi, Hudhayfa, [narrating] from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, Zayd ibn Thabit, Jarir ibn Abdullah, Abu Umama, Burayda al-Aslami, Abu Barza, Abdullah ibn al-Harith ibn Jaz al-Zubaidi — twenty-one men from the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, all of them narrated from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, concerning the affirmation of vision. So whoever rejects it has intended to deny these [Companions] in the hadiths and scriptural proofs.' And in this chapter there is abundance, and by these proofs the vision of Allah the Exalted has been established, and there is no establishment for that which is not possible, so it is established within the establishment the permissibility of vision.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage presents the third scriptural proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). It begins with a verse from Surah Yunus (10:26) where 'more' (ziyada) is interpreted by many early Muslims as the vision of Allah in the Hereafter. Nasafi argues that if the verse were interpreted otherwise, it would require diverting the apparent meaning without evidence, which is impermissible. He then states that this interpretation is supported by the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, as the belief in ru'ya was widespread among them. To substantiate this, he quotes al-Tirmidhi's work listing twenty-one Companions who narrated hadiths affirming ru'ya. The implication is that rejecting ru'ya amounts to rejecting these reliable narrators. Finally, Nasafi concludes that since these scriptural proofs establish the actual occurrence (thubut) of ru'ya, and something cannot occur if it is impossible, the permissibility (jawaz) is also necessarily established.

Key Terms
الرؤية (ru'ya)
زيادة (ziyada)
إجماع (ijma')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scriptural proofs only establish actual occurrence, not permissibility.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if something is established as occurring, it must be possible; thus permissibility is automatically proven.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the widespread narration of ru'ya hadiths among the Companions indicate?
A) That the hadiths are all authentic beyond doubt
B) That there is consensus (ijma') on the permissibility of ru'ya
C) That vision of Allah is a bodily vision
D) That the Quranic verse must be interpreted literally

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the Companions on ru'ya, combined with scripture, establishes both the occurrence and permissibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear that 21 of the Prophet's closest Companions all agreed on the reality of seeing Allah, does your heart truly accept their testimony, or do you harbor doubt? What in your life reveals that acceptance?
?
If the Companions' consensus is enough to establish a matter of the unseen, why do you still hesitate to submit your intellect to their authority in other matters of aqeedah? Will you today make a firm resolve to trust their consensus over your own reasoning?
?
The 'more' (ziyada) for those who do good is the vision of Allah. Have you ever lived your day knowing that the ultimate reward is to see your Lord? How does that awareness change what you do right now?

Proof from the Dying Person's Vision of the Angel of Death
Imam al-Nasafi presents a hadith-based proof for the visibility of unseen things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses the hadith about the dying person seeing the Angel of Death as a proof for the visibility of unseen things and the serious stance against those who reject such reports.

Core Idea
Hadith about the dying person's vision establishes that unseen things can be seen by God's will.

Imam al-Nasafi now presents a concrete, experiential proof from prophetic traditions: the dying person sees the Angel of Death while others present do not. This phenomenon, attested in hadith, supports the Maturidi position that unseen realities can be visible under certain conditions. The author emphasizes that denial of such narrations amounts to heresy, underscoring the centrality of transmitted knowledge in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the concise yet forceful style of Imam al-Nasafi's argument.
The passage is only a few lines but carries significant weight in establishing a proof from transmitted reports.
2
Understand the Proof Structure
Identify the three components: the observed phenomenon (dying person sees, others do not), the authoritative source (hadith), and the conclusion (supporting the Maturidi position). Consider why the author associates rejection with heresy.
This proof relies on the reliability of prophetic traditions, which are a foundational source of knowledge in Islamic theology.
3
Reflect on the Author's Strong Stance
Reflect on the implication that denying such hadith is linked to leaving Islam. This highlights the gravity of rejecting clearly transmitted reports from the Prophet (peace be upon him).
Consider how this view contrasts with purely rationalist approaches that might dismiss such reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا المحتضر يرى ملك الموت وأعوانه، ومن بحضرته من العواد والممرضين لا يرون شيئا من ذلك. ثبت هذا بأحاديث ينسب رادها وجاحدها إلى الإلحاد والخروج عن الإسلام. فدل أن ما ذهبنا إليه كلام صحيح لا يكاد يتوجه عليه سؤال لو تأمل الخصم وأنصف.
And likewise the dying person sees the Angel of Death and his helpers, while those present among the visitors and nurses do not see anything of that. This is established by hadiths whose rejecter and denier is attributed to heresy and leaving Islam. So it indicates that what we have held is correct speech upon which no objection can nearly be directed if the opponent were to reflect and be fair.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from transmitted reports (hadith) that the dying person sees the Angel of Death while others do not. The author argues that this shows the validity of their position regarding the potential visibility of certain things depending on God’s will. The denial of such hadiths is considered heretical.

Key Terms

الإلحاد

Key Takeaway
The dying person's vision of the Angel of Death, confirmed by hadith, serves as a proof that unseen things can be seen, and those who reject such reports are considered heretics.

?

Resurrection Possible: Initial Creation More Difficult
Al-Nasafi argues that initial creation is harder, so resurrection is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Nasafi argues that initial creation is more difficult than resurrection, making resurrection possible for God.

Core Idea
If God can create from nothing, He can certainly recreate.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi unpacks a Quranic response to those who deny resurrection. He shows that the very act of initial creation is more astonishing in human estimation than bringing bones back to life. This sets the stage for proving that resurrection is not impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Nasafi describes the denier's objection and God's response.
2
Identify the key analogy
Find the comparison between initial creation and re-creation. Why is the first more difficult in our minds?
The passage says 'the beginning is more difficult than the return'.
3
Understand the implication
If the more difficult act (initial creation) was possible, then the less difficult act (resurrection) is even more possible.
This places resurrection in the realm of possibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الله تعالى كما أخبر عن مقالة المنكر للبعث على الإجمال عقبه قبل إعادة التفسير ما يوجب كونه في حيز الممكنات، ثم أعاد ذكر المقالة وبيان إمكان البعث على طريق التفسير والإيضاح فقال تعالى: وَضَرَبَ لَنَا مَثَلًا وَنَسِيَ خَلْقَهُ تقديره: خلقه أولاً، والله أعلم، وضرب لنا مثلاً بقوله: (مَن يُحْيِ الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ) ونسي خلقه أن الله تعالى أنشأ أول مرة لا عن أصل والبداية في تقدير عقولنا أصعب من الإعادة، فعين بذلك أن ذلك ليس من جملة المستحيلات الممتنعات لأن ما هو أصعب في تقدير عقولكم كان ممكنا وتثبت قدرة الله بما عليه قدر ابتداء لأولى في تقدير عقولكم ثم بقوله تعالى: (وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمٌ) بين أن الإعادة وإن كان فعلاً محكماً لا تتأتى إلا من عالم فالله تعالى عالم بعلم يحيط بالمعلومات أجمع، ثم بقوله: ﴿ الَّذِي جَعَلَ لَكُم مِّنَ الشَّجَرِ الْأَخْضَرِ نَارًا ﴾ بين كمال قدرته وعجيب تدبيره أنه جعل من الشجر الأخضر الذي روى أصوله وفروعه وغصونه من الماء إذ جعل من الأخضر بدون الماء نارا مع تضاد في كيفيتهما، إذ الماء بارد رطب والنار حارة يابسة، ولم يصعب عليه ذلك لكمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وصائب تدبيره ولطف تقديره، وأعلم أن من له هذه القدرة والمشيئة وهذا التدبير والتقدير لا يبعد عنه إيجاد الحياة في العظام الرميمة والأجساد البالية، إذ ليس فيها ما يضاد الحياة، ولو كان فيها ذلك لكان قادرا على إعدام المضاد وإيجاد الحياة وذلك أقرب في تقدير عقولنا من جعل النار من الشجر الأخضر مع بقاء خضرته ووجود ما فيه من الماء، ثم أشار بقوله: أَوَلَيْسَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ
Then indeed Allah, the Exalted, as He informed about the statement of the denier of resurrection in general, He preceded before [the verse of] re-explanation what necessitates that it be within the realm of possibles, then He repeated the statement and the explanation of the possibility of resurrection in the way of interpretation and clarification, and He said, exalted: 'And he struck for Us an example and forgot his creation' – its implied meaning: his creation first, and Allah knows best – and he struck an example by His saying: 'Who will give life to bones while they are decayed?' And he forgot his creation that Allah, the Exalted, originated [it] first without an origin, and the beginning is in the estimation of our minds more difficult than the return. So He specified by that that it is not among the impossible and prevented things, because what is more difficult in the estimation of your minds was possible, and the power of Allah is established by what He was able to do from the beginning is more fitting in the estimation of your minds. Then by His saying, exalted: 'And He is of all creation Knowing' He explained that the return, even if it is a perfected act, cannot be achieved except from a knower, and Allah, the Exalted, is Knowing with knowledge that encompasses all known things. Then by His saying: 'He who made for you from the green tree fire' He explained the perfection of His power and the wonder of His administration, that He made from the green tree whose roots, branches, and twigs drank from water, as He made from the green without water fire, despite the opposition in their qualities, for water is cold and wet and fire is hot and dry, and that was not difficult for Him due to the perfection of His power, the penetration of His will, the correctness of His administration, and the subtlety of His estimation. And He made known that He who has this power, will, administration, and estimation, the giving of life to decayed bones and worn-out bodies is not far from Him, for there is nothing in them that opposes life, and if they had contained that, He would be able to remove the opposition and bring life, and that is closer in the estimation of our minds than making fire from green tree while preserving its greenness and the existence of water in it. Then He pointed by His saying: 'Is not He who created the heavens and the earth...'
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage is a theological exegesis combined with rational argument. Al-Nasafi first states the method: Allah first reported the denier's claim in general, then provided what makes it clear that resurrection belongs to the realm of possibles, then detailed it. He cites Quran 36:78: 'And he strikes for Us an example and forgets his creation' – the denier asks who can revive bones. Al-Nasafi explains the implicit point: the denier forgot that Allah created him first without any preexisting matter, and in human estimation, the first creation (ex nihilo) is more difficult than re-creation. Therefore, if that was possible, resurrection is even more possible. This shows that resurrection is not among the impossibilities previously listed. Next, Allah's attribute of omniscience (Quran 36:79) shows that the perfected act of re-creation requires knowledge, and Allah's knowledge encompasses all. Third, the example of fire from green tree (Quran 36:80) demonstrates God's power to produce opposites from opposites (fire from water-filled green wood). Since this is more astonishing than reviving bones, the power of God makes resurrection easy. Finally, the passage concludes with an argument a fortiori: if He can create the heavens and earth (Quran 46:33 hinted), He can certainly revive bones. The author thus weaves Quranic verses into a rational proof that resurrection is possible.

Key Terms
بعث
إعادة
ممكنات
مستحيلات
بداية
إحياء
رميم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resurrection is impossible because bones have decayed.
Correction: The passage shows that initial creation from nothing is more difficult, so if that was possible, resurrection is even more possible for God.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, what makes resurrection possible despite the decay of bones?
A) Bones can reassemble naturally over time.
B) Initial creation was more difficult, so re-creation is easier for God.
C) The denier forgot his own argument.
D) Fire from green tree is a natural phenomenon.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of resurrection is established by the fact that initial creation is more difficult in human estimation and yet occurred.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the denier forgot his own creation from nothing. When was the last time you genuinely paused to reflect that Allah created you from nothing? Does that truth truly settle in your heart, or do you also 'forget'? How would your daily life change if you lived with constant awareness that the One who brought you into existence from nothing is more than capable of bringing you back after death? Commit to a specific act this week to remind yourself of this: for example, recite 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' with full presence of mind at least once daily.
?
The passage argues that initial creation is more difficult in human estimation than resurrection. But do you actually believe in your heart that your own existence is proof that resurrection is just as possible? If yes, why does your life not reflect the urgency of preparing for that day? If no, what is the barrier in your heart that prevents full certainty? Take a few minutes right now to ask Allah to remove any doubt and grant you yaqeen (certainty).
?
Consider the denier's question: 'Who will give life to bones while they are decayed?' Are there any areas of your life—relationships, hopes, your own faith—that you have regarded as 'dead' or beyond revival? Is there something you have given up on that only Allah can revive? Repent for any despair of Allah's mercy and ask Him to resurrect what is dead in your life, trusting that He who created from nothing can restore anything.

Divine Knowledge and Power: Further Quranic Proofs for Resurrection
Al-Nasafi cites Quranic verses on God's knowledge and power to further prove resurrection possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi uses God's omniscience and the miracle of fire from green tree to prove that resurrection is not far from God's power.

Core Idea
God's knowledge and power make resurrection easy, even easier than making fire from water-filled wood.

After establishing the initial creation argument, Al-Nasafi presents two more Quranic proofs. First, God's knowledge encompasses all things, necessary for the perfected act of re-creation. Second, the example of fire from green tree demonstrates God's power over opposites, making resurrection seem straightforward. Finally, he hints at the even greater act of creating the heavens and earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: first the knowledge argument, then the power argument, then the final pointing.
2
Analyze the knowledge argument
Why does God need to be all-knowing for resurrection? What does 'perfected act' mean?
The passage says the return cannot be achieved except from a knower.
3
Understand the fire from green tree analogy
How does producing fire from a water-filled tree show God's power? It is an example of creating opposites from opposites.
Fire and water are opposites in qualities.
4
See the conclusion
If God can do that, then giving life to dead bones is even more likely, and nothing in bones opposes life.
The passage says 'He who has this power... giving life to decayed bones is not far from Him.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الله تعالى كما أخبر عن مقالة المنكر للبعث على الإجمال عقبه قبل إعادة التفسير ما يوجب كونه في حيز الممكنات، ثم أعاد ذكر المقالة وبيان إمكان البعث على طريق التفسير والإيضاح فقال تعالى: وَضَرَبَ لَنَا مَثَلًا وَنَسِيَ خَلْقَهُ تقديره: خلقه أولاً، والله أعلم، وضرب لنا مثلاً بقوله: (مَن يُحْيِ الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ) ونسي خلقه أن الله تعالى أنشأ أول مرة لا عن أصل والبداية في تقدير عقولنا أصعب من الإعادة، فعين بذلك أن ذلك ليس من جملة المستحيلات الممتنعات لأن ما هو أصعب في تقدير عقولكم كان ممكنا وتثبت قدرة الله بما عليه قدر ابتداء لأولى في تقدير عقولكم ثم بقوله تعالى: (وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمٌ) بين أن الإعادة وإن كان فعلاً محكماً لا تتأتى إلا من عالم فالله تعالى عالم بعلم يحيط بالمعلومات أجمع، ثم بقوله: ﴿ الَّذِي جَعَلَ لَكُم مِّنَ الشَّجَرِ الْأَخْضَرِ نَارًا ﴾ بين كمال قدرته وعجيب تدبيره أنه جعل من الشجر الأخضر الذي روى أصوله وفروعه وغصونه من الماء إذ جعل من الأخضر بدون الماء نارا مع تضاد في كيفيتهما، إذ الماء بارد رطب والنار حارة يابسة، ولم يصعب عليه ذلك لكمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وصائب تدبيره ولطف تقديره، وأعلم أن من له هذه القدرة والمشيئة وهذا التدبير والتقدير لا يبعد عنه إيجاد الحياة في العظام الرميمة والأجساد البالية، إذ ليس فيها ما يضاد الحياة، ولو كان فيها ذلك لكان قادرا على إعدام المضاد وإيجاد الحياة وذلك أقرب في تقدير عقولنا من جعل النار من الشجر الأخضر مع بقاء خضرته ووجود ما فيه من الماء، ثم أشار بقوله: أَوَلَيْسَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ
Then indeed Allah, the Exalted, as He informed about the statement of the denier of resurrection in general, He preceded before [the verse of] re-explanation what necessitates that it be within the realm of possibles, then He repeated the statement and the explanation of the possibility of resurrection in the way of interpretation and clarification, and He said, exalted: 'And he struck for Us an example and forgot his creation' – its implied meaning: his creation first, and Allah knows best – and he struck an example by His saying: 'Who will give life to bones while they are decayed?' And he forgot his creation that Allah, the Exalted, originated [it] first without an origin, and the beginning is in the estimation of our minds more difficult than the return. So He specified by that that it is not among the impossible and prevented things, because what is more difficult in the estimation of your minds was possible, and the power of Allah is established by what He was able to do from the beginning is more fitting in the estimation of your minds. Then by His saying, exalted: 'And He is of all creation Knowing' He explained that the return, even if it is a perfected act, cannot be achieved except from a knower, and Allah, the Exalted, is Knowing with knowledge that encompasses all known things. Then by His saying: 'He who made for you from the green tree fire' He explained the perfection of His power and the wonder of His administration, that He made from the green tree whose roots, branches, and twigs drank from water, as He made from the green without water fire, despite the opposition in their qualities, for water is cold and wet and fire is hot and dry, and that was not difficult for Him due to the perfection of His power, the penetration of His will, the correctness of His administration, and the subtlety of His estimation. And He made known that He who has this power, will, administration, and estimation, the giving of life to decayed bones and worn-out bodies is not far from Him, for there is nothing in them that opposes life, and if they had contained that, He would be able to remove the opposition and bring life, and that is closer in the estimation of our minds than making fire from green tree while preserving its greenness and the existence of water in it. Then He pointed by His saying: 'Is not He who created the heavens and the earth...'
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage is a theological exegesis combined with rational argument. Al-Nasafi first states the method: Allah first reported the denier's claim in general, then provided what makes it clear that resurrection belongs to the realm of possibles, then detailed it. He cites Quran 36:78: 'And he strikes for Us an example and forgets his creation' – the denier asks who can revive bones. Al-Nasafi explains the implicit point: the denier forgot that Allah created him first without any preexisting matter, and in human estimation, the first creation (ex nihilo) is more difficult than re-creation. Therefore, if that was possible, resurrection is even more possible. This shows that resurrection is not among the impossibilities previously listed. Next, Allah's attribute of omniscience (Quran 36:79) shows that the perfected act of re-creation requires knowledge, and Allah's knowledge encompasses all. Third, the example of fire from green tree (Quran 36:80) demonstrates God's power to produce opposites from opposites (fire from water-filled green wood). Since this is more astonishing than reviving bones, the power of God makes resurrection easy. Finally, the passage concludes with an argument a fortiori: if He can create the heavens and earth (Quran 46:33 hinted), He can certainly revive bones. The author thus weaves Quranic verses into a rational proof that resurrection is possible.

Key Terms
علم
قدرة
تضاد
بعث
إعادة
رميم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resurrection requires a natural explanation, like reassembling bones.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that resurrection is a direct act of God's power and knowledge, not a natural process.

Checkpoint
What does the example of fire from green tree demonstrate according to Al-Nasafi?
A) That fire can be produced from any wood.
B) That God can create opposites from opposites.
C) That water and fire are not opposites.
D) That green trees contain fire naturally.

Key Takeaway
God's omniscience and omnipotence, shown through Quranic examples, make resurrection not only possible but easy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God, who raised fire from water-filled wood, can resurrect your heart from its spiritual dryness and death? What specific area of your life feels as impossible as fire from wet wood? Will you today ask Him to do the seemingly impossible in that area?
?
When you look at your own moral failings and spiritual decay, do you see them as too far gone for God to restore? This lesson reminds you that God can give life to decayed bones. What one step of faith will you take today to act on that truth, rather than surrendering to hopelessness?
?
The passage argues that resurrection is easier than the miracle of fire from green tree. If you truly believed that, how would your view of death change? What would you do differently today if you were absolutely certain that God has the power to bring you back from any state?
